href="#cite_note-147">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Besides <a href="/wiki/Transfer_learning" title="Transfer learning">transfer learning</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> hypothetical AGI breakthroughs could include the development of reflective architectures that can engage in decision-theoretic metareasoning, and figuring out how to "slurp up" a comprehensive knowledge base from the entire unstructured <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">Web</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2009_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2009-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Some argue that some kind of (currently-undiscovered) conceptually straightforward, but mathematically difficult, "Master Algorithm" could lead to AGI.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDomingos2015_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDomingos2015-149">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Finally, a few "emergent" approaches look to simulating human intelligence extremely closely, and believe that <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic</a> features like an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">artificial brain</a> or simulated <a href="/wiki/Developmental_robotics" title="Developmental robotics">child development</a> may someday reach a critical point where general intelligence emerges.<sup id="cite_ref-Brain_simulation_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brain_simulation-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Many of the problems in this article may also require general intelligence, if machines are to solve the problems as well as people do. For example, even specific straightforward tasks, like <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">machine translation</a>, require that a machine read and write in both languages (<a href="#Natural_language_processing">NLP</a>), follow the author's argument (<a href="#Deduction,_reasoning,_problem_solving">reason</a>), know what is being talked about (<a href="#Knowledge_representation">knowledge</a>), and faithfully reproduce the author's original intent (<a href="#Social_intelligence">social intelligence</a>). A problem like machine translation is considered "<a href="/wiki/AI-complete" title="AI-complete">AI-complete</a>", because all of these problems need to be solved simultaneously in order to reach human-level machine performance.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches">Approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

There is no established unifying theory or <a href="/wiki/Paradigm" title="Paradigm">paradigm</a> that guides AI research. Researchers disagree about many issues.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> A few of the most long standing questions that have remained unanswered are these: should artificial intelligence simulate natural intelligence by studying <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neurobiology</a>? Or is <a href="/wiki/Human_biology" title="Human biology">human biology</a> as irrelevant to AI research as bird biology is to <a href="/wiki/Aeronautical_engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Aeronautical engineering">aeronautical engineering</a>?<sup id="cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
Can intelligent behavior be described using simple, elegant principles (such as <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Optimization (mathematics)">optimization</a>)? Or does it necessarily require solving a large number of completely unrelated problems?<sup id="cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cybernetics_and_brain_simulation">Cybernetics and brain simulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cybernetics and brain simulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">Cybernetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computational_neuroscience" title="Computational neuroscience">Computational neuroscience</a></div>

In the 1940s and 1950s, a number of researchers explored the connection between <a href="/wiki/Neurobiology" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurobiology">neurobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a>. Some of them built machines that used electronic networks to exhibit rudimentary intelligence, such as <a href="/wiki/W._Grey_Walter" class="mw-redirect" title="W. Grey Walter">W. Grey Walter</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Turtle_(robot)" title="Turtle (robot)">turtles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Beast" title="Johns Hopkins Beast">Johns Hopkins Beast</a>. Many of these researchers gathered for meetings of the Teleological Society at <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ratio_Club" title="Ratio Club">Ratio Club</a> in England.<sup id="cite_ref-AI&#39;s_immediate_precursors_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI&#39;s_immediate_precursors-153">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> By 1960, this approach was largely abandoned, although elements of it would be revived in the 1980s.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic">Symbolic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Symbolic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Symbolic_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Symbolic AI">Symbolic AI</a></div>

When access to digital computers became possible in the mid 1950s, AI research began to explore the possibility that human intelligence could be reduced to symbol manipulation. The research was centered in three institutions: <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanford" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanford">Stanford</a> and <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a>, and as described below, each one developed its own style of research. <a href="/wiki/John_Haugeland" title="John Haugeland">John Haugeland</a> named these symbolic approaches to AI "good old fashioned AI" or "<a href="/wiki/GOFAI" class="mw-redirect" title="GOFAI">GOFAI</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-GOFAI_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOFAI-154">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> During the 1960s, symbolic approaches had achieved great success at simulating high-level thinking in small demonstration programs. Approaches based on <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural networks</a> were abandoned or pushed into the background.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
Researchers in the 1960s and the 1970s were convinced that symbolic approaches would eventually succeed in creating a machine with <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">artificial general intelligence</a> and considered this the goal of their field.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_simulation">Cognitive simulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cognitive simulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

Economist <a href="/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon" title="Herbert A. Simon">Herbert Simon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allen_Newell" title="Allen Newell">Allen Newell</a> studied human problem-solving skills and attempted to formalize them, and their work laid the foundations of the field of artificial intelligence, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a> and <a href="/wiki/Management_science" title="Management science">management science</a>. Their research team used the results of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a> experiments to develop programs that simulated the techniques that people used to solve problems. This tradition, centered at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a> would eventually culminate in the development of the <a href="/wiki/Soar_(cognitive_architecture)" title="Soar (cognitive architecture)">Soar</a> architecture in the middle 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_at_CMU_in_the_60s_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_CMU_in_the_60s-156">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Soar_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soar-157">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic-based">Logic-based</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Logic-based">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

Unlike Simon and Newell, <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">John McCarthy</a> felt that machines did not need to simulate human thought, but should instead try to find the essence of abstract reasoning and problem-solving, regardless whether people used the same algorithms.<sup id="cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> His laboratory at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford</a> (<a href="/wiki/Stanford_Artificial_Intelligence_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanford Artificial Intelligence Laboratory">SAIL</a>) focused on using formal <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> to solve a wide variety of problems, including <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" class="mw-redirect" title="Knowledge representation">knowledge representation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_at_Stanford_in_the_60s_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_Stanford_in_the_60s-158">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Logic was also the focus of the work at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> and elsewhere in Europe which led to the development of the programming language <a href="/wiki/Prolog" title="Prolog">Prolog</a> and the science of <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">logic programming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_at_Edinburgh_and_France_in_the_60s_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_Edinburgh_and_France_in_the_60s-159">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-logic_or_scruffy">Anti-logic or scruffy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Anti-logic or scruffy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

Researchers at <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Papert" title="Seymour Papert">Seymour Papert</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-AI_at_MIT_in_the_60s_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_MIT_in_the_60s-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> found that solving difficult problems in <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">vision</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">natural language processing</a> required ad-hoc solutions—they argued that there was no simple and general principle (like <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>) that would capture all the aspects of intelligent behavior. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Schank" title="Roger Schank">Roger Schank</a> described their "anti-logic" approaches as "<a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" class="mw-redirect" title="Neats vs. scruffies">scruffy</a>" (as opposed to the "<a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" class="mw-redirect" title="Neats vs. scruffies">neat</a>" paradigms at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">CMU</a> and Stanford).<sup id="cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge_bases" class="mw-redirect" title="Commonsense knowledge bases">Commonsense knowledge bases</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Doug_Lenat" class="mw-redirect" title="Doug Lenat">Doug Lenat</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Cyc" title="Cyc">Cyc</a>) are an example of "scruffy" AI, since they must be built by hand, one complicated concept at a time.<sup id="cite_ref-Cyc_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cyc-161">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Knowledge-based">Knowledge-based</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Knowledge-based">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

When computers with large memories became available around 1970, researchers from all three traditions began to build <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" class="mw-redirect" title="Knowledge representation">knowledge</a> into AI applications.<sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_revolution_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_revolution-162">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> This "knowledge revolution" led to the development and deployment of <a href="/wiki/Expert_system" title="Expert system">expert systems</a> (introduced by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Feigenbaum" title="Edward Feigenbaum">Edward Feigenbaum</a>), the first truly successful form of AI software.<sup id="cite_ref-Expert_systems_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Expert_systems-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> A key component of the system architecture for all expert systems is the knowledge base, which stores facts and rules that illustrate AI.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> The knowledge revolution was also driven by the realization that enormous amounts of knowledge would be required by many simple AI applications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-symbolic">Sub-symbolic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sub-symbolic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

By the 1980s, progress in symbolic AI seemed to stall and many believed that symbolic systems would never be able to imitate all the processes of human cognition, especially <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition" title="Pattern recognition">pattern recognition</a>. A number of researchers began to look into "sub-symbolic" approaches to specific AI problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Sub-symbolic methods manage to approach intelligence without specific representations of knowledge.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Embodied_intelligence">Embodied intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Embodied intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

This includes <a href="/wiki/Embodied_agent" title="Embodied agent">embodied</a>, <a href="/wiki/Situated" title="Situated">situated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Behavior-based_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Behavior-based AI">behavior-based</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nouvelle_AI" title="Nouvelle AI">nouvelle AI</a>. Researchers from the related field of <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Brooks" title="Rodney Brooks">Rodney Brooks</a>, rejected symbolic AI and focused on the basic engineering problems that would allow robots to move and survive.<sup id="cite_ref-Embodied_AI_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Embodied_AI-164">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Their work revived the non-symbolic point of view of the early <a href="/wiki/Cybernetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cybernetic">cybernetics</a> researchers of the 1950s and reintroduced the use of <a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">control theory</a> in AI. This coincided with the development of the <a href="/wiki/Embodied_mind_thesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Embodied mind thesis">embodied mind thesis</a> in the related field of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>: the idea that aspects of the body (such as movement, perception and visualization) are required for higher intelligence.
</p>
Within <a href="/wiki/Developmental_robotics" title="Developmental robotics">developmental robotics</a>, developmental learning approaches are elaborated upon to allow robots to accumulate repertoires of novel skills through autonomous self-exploration, social interaction with human teachers, and the use of guidance mechanisms (active learning, maturation, motor synergies, etc.).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWengMcClellandPentlandSporns2001_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWengMcClellandPentlandSporns2001-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELungarellaMettaPfeiferSandini2003_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELungarellaMettaPfeiferSandini2003-166">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsadaHosodaKuniyoshiIshiguro2009_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsadaHosodaKuniyoshiIshiguro2009-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOudeyer2010_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOudeyer2010-168">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Computational_intelligence_and_soft_computing">Computational intelligence and soft computing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Computational intelligence and soft computing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

Interest in <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">neural networks</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">connectionism</a>" was revived by <a href="/wiki/David_Rumelhart" title="David Rumelhart">David Rumelhart</a> and others in the middle of the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Revival_of_connectionism_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Revival_of_connectionism-169">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">Artificial neural networks</a> are an example of <a href="/wiki/Soft_computing" title="Soft computing">soft computing</a>—they are solutions to problems which cannot be solved with complete logical certainty, and where an approximate solution is often sufficient. Other <a href="/wiki/Soft_computing" title="Soft computing">soft computing</a> approaches to AI include <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuzzy system">fuzzy systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grey_system_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Grey system theory">Grey system theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">evolutionary computation</a> and many statistical tools. The application of soft computing to AI is studied collectively by the emerging discipline of <a href="/wiki/Computational_intelligence" title="Computational intelligence">computational intelligence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Computational_intelligence_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computational_intelligence-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistical_learning">Statistical learning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Statistical learning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

Much of traditional <a href="/wiki/Symbolic_artificial_intelligence" title="Symbolic artificial intelligence">GOFAI</a> got bogged down on <i>ad hoc</i> patches to symbolic computation that worked on their own toy models but failed to generalize to real-world results. However, around the 1990s, AI researchers adopted sophisticated mathematical tools, such as <a href="/wiki/Hidden_Markov_model" title="Hidden Markov model">hidden Markov models</a> (HMM), <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>, and normative Bayesian <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a> to compare or to unify competing architectures. The shared mathematical language permitted a high level of collaboration with more established fields (like <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, economics or <a href="/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> Compared with GOFAI, new "statistical learning" techniques such as HMM and neural networks were gaining higher levels of accuracy in many practical domains such as <a href="/wiki/Data_mining" title="Data mining">data mining</a>, without necessarily acquiring a semantic understanding of the datasets. The increased successes with real-world data led to increasing emphasis on comparing different approaches against shared test data to see which approach performed best in a broader context than that provided by idiosyncratic toy models; AI research was becoming more <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific</a>. Nowadays results of experiments are often rigorously measurable, and are sometimes (with difficulty) reproducible.<sup id="cite_ref-Formal_methods_in_AI_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Formal_methods_in_AI-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Different statistical learning techniques have different limitations; for example, basic HMM cannot model the infinite possible combinations of natural language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorvig2012_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorvig2012-173">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Critics note that the shift from GOFAI to statistical learning is often also a shift away from <a href="/wiki/Explainable_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Explainable AI">explainable AI</a>. In AGI research, some scholars caution against over-reliance on statistical learning, and argue that continuing research into GOFAI will still be necessary to attain general intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangley2011_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangley2011-174">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKatz2012_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKatz2012-175">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrating_the_approaches">Integrating the approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Integrating the approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dt>Intelligent agent paradigm</dt>
<dd>An <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agent" title="Intelligent agent">intelligent agent</a> is a system that perceives its environment and takes actions which maximize its chances of success. The simplest intelligent agents are programs that solve specific problems. More complicated agents include human beings and organizations of human beings (such as <a href="/wiki/Firm" class="mw-redirect" title="Firm">firms</a>). The paradigm allows researchers to directly compare or even combine different approaches to isolated problems, by asking which agent is best at maximizing a given "goal function". An agent that solves a specific problem can use any approach that works—some agents are symbolic and logical, some are sub-symbolic <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural networks</a> and others may use new approaches. The paradigm also gives researchers a common language to communicate with other fields—such as <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a> and economics—that also use concepts of abstract agents. Building a complete agent requires researchers to address realistic problems of integration; for example, because sensory systems give uncertain information about the environment, planning systems must be able to function in the presence of uncertainty. The intelligent agent paradigm became widely accepted during the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-Intelligent_agents_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intelligent_agents-176">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Agent_architecture" title="Agent architecture">Agent architectures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_architecture" title="Cognitive architecture">cognitive architectures</a></dt>
<dd>Researchers have designed systems to build intelligent systems out of interacting <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agents" class="mw-redirect" title="Intelligent agents">intelligent agents</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Multi-agent_system" title="Multi-agent system">multi-agent system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Agent_architectures_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agent_architectures-177">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_control_system" title="Hierarchical control system">hierarchical control system</a> provides a bridge between sub-symbolic AI at its lowest, reactive levels and traditional symbolic AI at its highest levels, where relaxed time constraints permit planning and world modelling.<sup id="cite_ref-Hierarchical_control_system_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hierarchical_control_system-178">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Some cognitive architectures are custom-built to solve a narrow problem; others, such as <a href="/wiki/Soar_(cognitive_architecture)" title="Soar (cognitive architecture)">Soar</a>, are designed to mimic human cognition and to provide insight into general intelligence. Modern extensions of Soar are <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_intelligent_system" title="Hybrid intelligent system">hybrid intelligent systems</a> that include both symbolic and sub-symbolic components.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tools">Tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

AI has developed a large number of tools to solve the most difficult problems in <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>. A few of the most general of these methods are discussed below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_and_optimization">Search and optimization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Search and optimization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Search_algorithm" title="Search algorithm">Search algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_optimization" title="Mathematical optimization">Mathematical optimization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">Evolutionary computation</a></div>

Many problems in AI can be solved in theory by intelligently searching through many possible solutions:<sup id="cite_ref-Search_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Search-182">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> <a href="#Deduction,_reasoning,_problem_solving">Reasoning</a> can be reduced to performing a search. For example, logical proof can be viewed as searching for a path that leads from <a href="/wiki/Premise" title="Premise">premises</a> to <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence">conclusions</a>, where each step is the application of an <a href="/wiki/Inference_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Inference rule">inference rule</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Logic_as_search_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Logic_as_search-183">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">Planning</a> algorithms search through trees of goals and subgoals, attempting to find a path to a target goal, a process called <a href="/wiki/Means-ends_analysis" title="Means-ends analysis">means-ends analysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Planning_as_search_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Planning_as_search-184">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">Robotics</a> algorithms for moving limbs and grasping objects use <a href="/wiki/Local_search_(optimization)" title="Local search (optimization)">local searches</a> in <a href="/wiki/Configuration_space_(physics)" title="Configuration space (physics)">configuration space</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Configuration_space_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Configuration_space-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> algorithms use search algorithms based on <a href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Optimization (mathematics)">optimization</a>.
</p>
Simple exhaustive searches<sup id="cite_ref-Uninformed_search_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uninformed_search-185">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> are rarely sufficient for most real-world problems: the <a href="/wiki/Search_algorithm" title="Search algorithm">search space</a> (the number of places to search) quickly grows to <a href="/wiki/Astronomically_large" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomically large">astronomical numbers</a>. The result is a search that is <a href="/wiki/Computation_time" class="mw-redirect" title="Computation time">too slow</a> or never completes. The solution, for many problems, is to use "<a href="/wiki/Heuristics" class="mw-redirect" title="Heuristics">heuristics</a>" or "rules of thumb" that prioritize choices in favor of those that are more likely to reach a goal and to do so in a shorter number of steps. In some search methodologies heuristics can also serve to entirely eliminate some choices that are unlikely to lead to a goal (called "<a href="/wiki/Pruning_(algorithm)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pruning (algorithm)">pruning</a> the <a href="/wiki/Search_tree" title="Search tree">search tree</a>"). <a href="/wiki/Heuristics" class="mw-redirect" title="Heuristics">Heuristics</a> supply the program with a "best guess" for the path on which the solution lies.<sup id="cite_ref-Informed_search_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Informed_search-186">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Heuristics limit the search for solutions into a smaller sample size.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETecuci2012_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETecuci2012-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
A very different kind of search came to prominence in the 1990s, based on the mathematical theory of <a href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Optimization (mathematics)">optimization</a>. For many problems, it is possible to begin the search with some form of a guess and then refine the guess incrementally until no more refinements can be made. These algorithms can be visualized as blind <a href="/wiki/Hill_climbing" title="Hill climbing">hill climbing</a>: we begin the search at a random point on the landscape, and then, by jumps or steps, we keep moving our guess uphill, until we reach the top. Other optimization algorithms are <a href="/wiki/Simulated_annealing" title="Simulated annealing">simulated annealing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beam_search" title="Beam search">beam search</a> and <a href="/wiki/Random_optimization" title="Random optimization">random optimization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Optimization_search_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Optimization_search-187">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ParticleSwarmArrowsAnimation.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ParticleSwarmArrowsAnimation.gif/220px-ParticleSwarmArrowsAnimation.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ParticleSwarmArrowsAnimation.gif/330px-ParticleSwarmArrowsAnimation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ParticleSwarmArrowsAnimation.gif/440px-ParticleSwarmArrowsAnimation.gif 2x" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ParticleSwarmArrowsAnimation.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Particle_swarm_optimization" title="Particle swarm optimization">particle swarm</a> seeking the <a href="/wiki/Global_minimum" class="mw-redirect" title="Global minimum">global minimum</a></div></div></div>

<a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">Evolutionary computation</a> uses a form of optimization search. For example, they may begin with a population of organisms (the guesses) and then allow them to mutate and recombine, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_selection" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial selection">selecting</a> only the fittest to survive each generation (refining the guesses). Classic <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Evolutionary algorithms">evolutionary algorithms</a> include <a href="/wiki/Genetic_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetic algorithms">genetic algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression_programming" title="Gene expression programming">gene expression programming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_programming" title="Genetic programming">genetic programming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetic_programming_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetic_programming-188">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Alternatively, distributed search processes can coordinate via <a href="/wiki/Swarm_intelligence" title="Swarm intelligence">swarm intelligence</a> algorithms. Two popular swarm algorithms used in search are <a href="/wiki/Particle_swarm_optimization" title="Particle swarm optimization">particle swarm optimization</a> (inspired by bird <a href="/wiki/Flocking_(behavior)" title="Flocking (behavior)">flocking</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ant_colony_optimization" class="mw-redirect" title="Ant colony optimization">ant colony optimization</a> (inspired by <a href="/wiki/Ant_trail" class="mw-redirect" title="Ant trail">ant trails</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Society_based_learning_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Society_based_learning-189">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">Logic programming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Automated_reasoning" title="Automated reasoning">Automated reasoning</a></div>

<a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Logic</a><sup id="cite_ref-Logic_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Logic-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> is used for knowledge representation and problem solving, but it can be applied to other problems as well. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Satplan" title="Satplan">satplan</a> algorithm uses logic for <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a><sup id="cite_ref-Satplan_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satplan-192">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_logic_programming" title="Inductive logic programming">inductive logic programming</a> is a method for <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Symbolic_learning_techniques_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Symbolic_learning_techniques-193">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Several different forms of logic are used in AI research. <a href="/wiki/Propositional_logic" class="mw-redirect" title="Propositional logic">Propositional logic</a><sup id="cite_ref-Propositional_logic_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Propositional_logic-194">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> involves <a href="/wiki/Truth_function" title="Truth function">truth functions</a> such as "or" and "not". <a href="/wiki/First-order_logic" title="First-order logic">First-order logic</a><sup id="cite_ref-First-order_logic_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-First-order_logic-195">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> adds <a href="/wiki/Quantifier_(logic)" title="Quantifier (logic)">quantifiers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(mathematical_logic)" title="Predicate (mathematical logic)">predicates</a>, and can express facts about objects, their properties, and their relations with each other. <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_set_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuzzy set theory">Fuzzy set theory</a> assigns a "degree of truth" (between 0 and 1) to vague statements such as "Alice is old" (or rich, or tall, or hungry) that are too linguistically imprecise to be completely true or false. <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_logic" title="Fuzzy logic">Fuzzy logic</a> is successfully used in <a href="/wiki/Control_system" title="Control system">control systems</a> to allow experts to contribute vague rules such as "if you are close to the destination station and moving fast, increase the train's brake pressure"; these vague rules can then be numerically refined within the system. Fuzzy logic fails to scale well in knowledge bases; many AI researchers question the validity of chaining fuzzy-logic inferences.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fuzzy_logic_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuzzy_logic-198">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Default_logic" title="Default logic">Default logics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-monotonic_logic" title="Non-monotonic logic">non-monotonic logics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circumscription_(logic)" title="Circumscription (logic)">circumscription</a><sup id="cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> are forms of logic designed to help with default reasoning and the <a href="/wiki/Qualification_problem" title="Qualification problem">qualification problem</a>. Several extensions of logic have been designed to handle specific domains of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" class="mw-redirect" title="Knowledge representation">knowledge</a>, such as: <a href="/wiki/Description_logic" title="Description logic">description logics</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_categories_and_relations_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_categories_and_relations-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Situation_calculus" title="Situation calculus">situation calculus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Event_calculus" title="Event calculus">event calculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluent_calculus" title="Fluent calculus">fluent calculus</a> (for representing events and time);<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_time_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_time-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Causality#Causal_calculus" title="Causality">causal calculus</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_causation_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_causation-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> belief calculus;<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Overall, qualitative symbolic logic is brittle and scales poorly in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Noise_(signal_processing)" title="Noise (signal processing)">noise</a> or other uncertainty. Exceptions to rules are numerous, and it is difficult for logical systems to function in the presence of contradictory rules.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDomingos2015chapter_6_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDomingos2015chapter_6-201">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Probabilistic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning">Probabilistic methods for uncertain reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Probabilistic methods for uncertain reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_network" title="Bayesian network">Bayesian network</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hidden_Markov_model" title="Hidden Markov model">Hidden Markov model</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalman_filter" title="Kalman filter">Kalman filter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Particle_filter" title="Particle filter">Particle filter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">Decision theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Utility_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Utility theory">Utility theory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EM_Clustering_of_Old_Faithful_data.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/69/EM_Clustering_of_Old_Faithful_data.gif" decoding="async" width="360" height="309" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="309" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Expectation-maximization" class="mw-redirect" title="Expectation-maximization">Expectation-maximization</a> clustering of <a href="/wiki/Old_Faithful" title="Old Faithful">Old Faithful</a> eruption data starts from a random guess but then successfully converges on an accurate clustering of the two physically distinct modes of eruption.</div></div></div>

Many problems in AI (in reasoning, planning, learning, perception, and robotics) require the agent to operate with incomplete or uncertain information. AI researchers have devised a number of powerful tools to solve these problems using methods from <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> theory and economics.<sup id="cite_ref-Stochastic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning-203">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Bayesian_network" title="Bayesian network">Bayesian networks</a><sup id="cite_ref-Bayesian_networks_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_networks-204">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> are a very general tool that can be used for a large number of problems: reasoning (using the <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian inference</a> algorithm),<sup id="cite_ref-Bayesian_inference_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_inference-205">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> (using the <a href="/wiki/Expectation-maximization_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Expectation-maximization algorithm">expectation-maximization algorithm</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bayesian_learning_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_learning-208">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a> (using <a href="/wiki/Decision_network" class="mw-redirect" title="Decision network">decision networks</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Bayesian_decision_networks_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_decision_networks-209">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a> (using <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_Bayesian_network" title="Dynamic Bayesian network">dynamic Bayesian networks</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-210">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Probabilistic algorithms can also be used for filtering, prediction, smoothing and finding explanations for streams of data, helping <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a> systems to analyze processes that occur over time (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Hidden_Markov_model" title="Hidden Markov model">hidden Markov models</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kalman_filter" title="Kalman filter">Kalman filters</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-210">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Compared with symbolic logic, formal Bayesian inference is computationally expensive. For inference to be tractable, most observations must be <a href="/wiki/Conditionally_independent" class="mw-redirect" title="Conditionally independent">conditionally independent</a> of one another. Complicated graphs with diamonds or other "loops" (undirected <a href="/wiki/Cycle_(graph_theory)" title="Cycle (graph theory)">cycles</a>) can require a sophisticated method such as <a href="/wiki/Markov_chain_Monte_Carlo" title="Markov chain Monte Carlo">Markov chain Monte Carlo</a>, which spreads an ensemble of <a href="/wiki/Random_walk" title="Random walk">random walkers</a> throughout the Bayesian network and attempts to converge to an assessment of the conditional probabilities. Bayesian networks are used on <a href="/wiki/Xbox_Live" title="Xbox Live">Xbox Live</a> to rate and match players; wins and losses are "evidence" of how good a player is<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. <a href="/wiki/AdSense" class="mw-redirect" title="AdSense">AdSense</a> uses a Bayesian network with over 300 million edges to learn which ads to serve.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDomingos2015chapter_6_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDomingos2015chapter_6-201">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
A key concept from the science of economics is "<a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a>": a measure of how valuable something is to an intelligent agent. Precise mathematical tools have been developed that analyze how an agent can make choices and plan, using <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision_analysis" title="Decision analysis">decision analysis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Decisions_theory_and_analysis_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Decisions_theory_and_analysis-211">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Applied_information_economics" title="Applied information economics">information value theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Information_value_theory_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Information_value_theory-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> These tools include models such as <a href="/wiki/Markov_decision_process" title="Markov decision process">Markov decision processes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Markov_decision_process_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markov_decision_process-212">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> dynamic <a href="/wiki/Decision_network" class="mw-redirect" title="Decision network">decision networks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_210-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-210">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_design" title="Mechanism design">mechanism design</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Game_theory_and_mechanism_design_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Game_theory_and_mechanism_design-213">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifiers_and_statistical_learning_methods">Classifiers and statistical learning methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Classifiers and statistical learning methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Classifier_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Classifier (mathematics)">Classifier (mathematics)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statistical_classification" title="Statistical classification">Statistical classification</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">Machine learning</a></div>

The simplest AI applications can be divided into two types: classifiers ("if shiny then diamond") and controllers ("if shiny then pick up"). Controllers do, however, also classify conditions before inferring actions, and therefore classification forms a central part of many AI systems. <a href="/wiki/Classifier_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Classifier (mathematics)">Classifiers</a> are functions that use <a href="/wiki/Pattern_matching" title="Pattern matching">pattern matching</a> to determine a closest match. They can be tuned according to examples, making them very attractive for use in AI. These examples are known as observations or patterns. In supervised learning, each pattern belongs to a certain predefined class. A class can be seen as a decision that has to be made. All the observations combined with their class labels are known as a data set. When a new observation is received, that observation is classified based on previous experience.<sup id="cite_ref-Classifiers_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Classifiers-214">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
A classifier can be trained in various ways; there are many statistical and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> approaches. The <a href="/wiki/Decision_tree_learning" title="Decision tree learning">decision tree</a><sup id="cite_ref-Decision_tree_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Decision_tree-215">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> is perhaps the most widely used machine learning algorithm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDomingos201588_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDomingos201588-216">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Other widely used classifiers are the <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">neural network</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Neural_networks_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neural_networks-217">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="K-nearest neighbor algorithm">k-nearest neighbor algorithm</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm-220">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Kernel_methods" class="mw-redirect" title="Kernel methods">kernel methods</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Support_vector_machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Support vector machine">support vector machine</a> (SVM),<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kernel_methods_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kernel_methods-223">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Gaussian_mixture_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaussian mixture model">Gaussian mixture model</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Gaussian_mixture_model_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaussian_mixture_model-224">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> and the extremely popular <a href="/wiki/Naive_Bayes_classifier" title="Naive Bayes classifier">naive Bayes classifier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naive_Bayes_classifier_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naive_Bayes_classifier-227">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Classifier performance depends greatly on the characteristics of the data to be classified, such as the dataset size, distribution of samples across classes, the dimensionality, and the level of noise. Model-based classifiers perform well if the assumed model is an extremely good fit for the actual data. Otherwise, if no matching model is available, and if accuracy (rather than speed or scalability) is the sole concern, conventional wisdom is that discriminative classifiers (especially SVM) tend to be more accurate than model-based classifiers such as "naive Bayes" on most practical data sets.<sup id="cite_ref-Classifier_performance_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Classifier_performance-228">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200918.12:_Learning_from_Examples:_Summary_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200918.12:_Learning_from_Examples:_Summary-229">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial_neural_networks">Artificial neural networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Artificial neural networks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">Artificial neural network</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">Connectionism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Artificial_neural_network.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Artificial_neural_network.svg/220px-Artificial_neural_network.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Artificial_neural_network.svg/330px-Artificial_neural_network.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Artificial_neural_network.svg/440px-Artificial_neural_network.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Artificial_neural_network.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A neural network is an interconnected group of nodes, akin to the vast network of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a>.</div></div></div>

Neural networks were inspired by the architecture of neurons in the human brain. A simple "neuron" <i>N</i> accepts input from multiple other neurons, each of which, when activated (or "fired"), cast a weighted "vote" for or against whether neuron <i>N</i> should itself activate. Learning requires an algorithm to adjust these weights based on the training data; one simple algorithm (dubbed "<a href="/wiki/Hebbian_learning" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebbian learning">fire together, wire together</a>") is to increase the weight between two connected neurons when the activation of one triggers the successful activation of another. The neural network forms "concepts" that are distributed among a subnetwork of shared<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;j&#93;</a></sup> neurons that tend to fire together; a concept meaning "leg" might be coupled with a subnetwork meaning "foot" that includes the sound for "foot". Neurons have a continuous spectrum of activation; in addition, neurons can process inputs in a nonlinear way rather than weighing straightforward votes. Modern neural networks can learn both continuous functions and, surprisingly, digital logical operations. Neural networks' early successes included predicting the stock market and (in 1995) a mostly self-driving car.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;k&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDomingos2015Chapter_4_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDomingos2015Chapter_4-232">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> In the 2010s, advances in neural networks using deep learning thrust AI into widespread public consciousness and contributed to an enormous upshift in corporate AI spending; for example, AI-related <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">M&amp;A</a> in 2017 was over 25 times as large as in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
The study of non-learning <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural networks</a><sup id="cite_ref-Neural_networks_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neural_networks-217">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> began in the decade before the field of AI research was founded, in the work of <a href="/wiki/Walter_Pitts" title="Walter Pitts">Walter Pitts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warren_McCullouch" class="mw-redirect" title="Warren McCullouch">Warren McCullouch</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Rosenblatt" title="Frank Rosenblatt">Frank Rosenblatt</a> invented the <a href="/wiki/Perceptron" title="Perceptron">perceptron</a>, a learning network with a single layer, similar to the old concept of <a href="/wiki/Linear_regression" title="Linear regression">linear regression</a>. Early pioneers also include <a href="/wiki/Alexey_Grigorevich_Ivakhnenko" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexey Grigorevich Ivakhnenko">Alexey Grigorevich Ivakhnenko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teuvo_Kohonen" title="Teuvo Kohonen">Teuvo Kohonen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Grossberg" title="Stephen Grossberg">Stephen Grossberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunihiko_Fukushima" title="Kunihiko Fukushima">Kunihiko Fukushima</a>, Christoph von der Malsburg, David Willshaw, <a href="/wiki/Shun-Ichi_Amari" class="mw-redirect" title="Shun-Ichi Amari">Shun-Ichi Amari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Widrow" title="Bernard Widrow">Bernard Widrow</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Hopfield" title="John Hopfield">John Hopfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eduardo_R._Caianiello" title="Eduardo R. Caianiello">Eduardo R. Caianiello</a>, and others<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.
</p>
The main categories of networks are acyclic or <a href="/wiki/Feedforward_neural_network" title="Feedforward neural network">feedforward neural networks</a> (where the signal passes in only one direction) and <a href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_network" title="Recurrent neural network">recurrent neural networks</a> (which allow feedback and short-term memories of previous input events). Among the most popular feedforward networks are <a href="/wiki/Perceptron" title="Perceptron">perceptrons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multi-layer_perceptron" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-layer perceptron">multi-layer perceptrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radial_basis_network" class="mw-redirect" title="Radial basis network">radial basis networks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Feedforward_neural_networks_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feedforward_neural_networks-235">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Neural networks can be applied to the problem of <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_control" title="Intelligent control">intelligent control</a> (for robotics) or <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>, using such techniques as <a href="/wiki/Hebbian_learning" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebbian learning">Hebbian learning</a> ("fire together, wire together"), <a href="/wiki/GMDH" class="mw-redirect" title="GMDH">GMDH</a> or <a href="/wiki/Competitive_learning" title="Competitive learning">competitive learning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Learning_in_neural_networks_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learning_in_neural_networks-236">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Today, neural networks are often trained by the <a href="/wiki/Backpropagation" title="Backpropagation">backpropagation</a> algorithm, which had been around since 1970 as the reverse mode of <a href="/wiki/Automatic_differentiation" title="Automatic differentiation">automatic differentiation</a> published by <a href="/wiki/Seppo_Linnainmaa" title="Seppo Linnainmaa">Seppo Linnainmaa</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-lin1970_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lin1970-237">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-grie2012_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grie2012-238">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> and was introduced to neural networks by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Werbos" title="Paul Werbos">Paul Werbos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WERBOS1974_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WERBOS1974-239">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-werbos1982_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-werbos1982-240">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Backpropagation_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Backpropagation-241">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_temporal_memory" title="Hierarchical temporal memory">Hierarchical temporal memory</a> is an approach that models some of the structural and algorithmic properties of the <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hierarchical_temporal_memory_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hierarchical_temporal_memory-242">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
To summarize, most neural networks use some form of <a href="/wiki/Gradient_descent" title="Gradient descent">gradient descent</a> on a hand-created neural topology. However, some research groups, such as <a href="/wiki/Uber" title="Uber">Uber</a>, argue that simple <a href="/wiki/Neuroevolution" title="Neuroevolution">neuroevolution</a> to mutate new neural network topologies and weights may be competitive with sophisticated gradient descent approaches<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. One advantage of neuroevolution is that it may be less prone to get caught in "dead ends".<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deep_feedforward_neural_networks">Deep feedforward neural networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Deep feedforward neural networks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deep_learning" title="Deep learning">Deep learning</a></div>

<a href="/wiki/Deep_learning" title="Deep learning">Deep learning</a> is any <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural network</a> that can learn a long chain of causal links<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (July 2019)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Artificial_intelligence#Dubious" title="Talk:Artificial intelligence">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup>. For example, a feedforward network with six hidden layers can learn a seven-link causal chain (six hidden layers + output layer) and has a <a href="/wiki/Deep_learning#Credit_assignment" title="Deep learning">"credit assignment path"</a> (CAP) depth of seven<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. Many deep learning systems need to be able to learn chains ten or more causal links in length.<sup id="cite_ref-schmidhuber2015_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmidhuber2015-244">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Deep learning has transformed many important subfields of artificial intelligence<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The reason for this is unclear. (July 2019)">why?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, including <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">computer vision</a>, <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">speech recognition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">natural language processing</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-goodfellow2016_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodfellow2016-245">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HintonDengYu2012_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HintonDengYu2012-246">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-schmidhuber2015_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmidhuber2015-244">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
According to one overview,<sup id="cite_ref-scholarpedia_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scholarpedia-247">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> the expression "Deep Learning" was introduced to the <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> community by <a href="/wiki/Rina_Dechter" title="Rina Dechter">Rina Dechter</a> in 1986<sup id="cite_ref-dechter1986_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dechter1986-248">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> and gained traction after
Igor Aizenberg and colleagues introduced it to <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural networks</a> in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-aizenberg2000_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aizenberg2000-249">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The first functional Deep Learning networks were published by <a href="/wiki/Alexey_Grigorevich_Ivakhnenko" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexey Grigorevich Ivakhnenko">Alexey Grigorevich Ivakhnenko</a> and V. G. Lapa in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2016)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> These networks are trained one layer at a time. Ivakhnenko's 1971 paper<sup id="cite_ref-ivak1971_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ivak1971-251">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> describes the learning of a deep feedforward multilayer perceptron with eight layers, already much deeper than many later networks. In 2006, a publication by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton" title="Geoffrey Hinton">Geoffrey Hinton</a> and Ruslan Salakhutdinov introduced another way of pre-training many-layered <a href="/wiki/Feedforward_neural_network" title="Feedforward neural network">feedforward neural networks</a> (FNNs) one layer at a time, treating each layer in turn as an <a href="/wiki/Unsupervised_learning" title="Unsupervised learning">unsupervised</a> <a href="/wiki/Restricted_Boltzmann_machine" title="Restricted Boltzmann machine">restricted Boltzmann machine</a>, then using <a href="/wiki/Supervised_learning" title="Supervised learning">supervised</a> <a href="/wiki/Backpropagation" title="Backpropagation">backpropagation</a> for fine-tuning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinton2007_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinton2007-252">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Similar to shallow artificial neural networks, deep neural networks can model complex non-linear relationships. Over the last few years, advances in both machine learning algorithms and computer hardware have led to more efficient methods for training deep neural networks that contain many layers of non-linear hidden units and a very large output layer.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Deep learning often uses <a href="/wiki/Convolutional_neural_network" title="Convolutional neural network">convolutional neural networks</a> (CNNs), whose origins can be traced back to the <a href="/wiki/Neocognitron" title="Neocognitron">Neocognitron</a> introduced by <a href="/wiki/Kunihiko_Fukushima" title="Kunihiko Fukushima">Kunihiko Fukushima</a> in 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-FUKU1980_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FUKU1980-254">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> In 1989, <a href="/wiki/Yann_LeCun" title="Yann LeCun">Yann LeCun</a> and colleagues applied <a href="/wiki/Backpropagation" title="Backpropagation">backpropagation</a> to such an architecture. In the early 2000s, in an industrial application CNNs already processed an estimated 10% to 20% of all the checks written in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-lecun2016slides_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lecun2016slides-255">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>
Since 2011, fast implementations of CNNs on GPUs have
won many visual pattern recognition competitions.<sup id="cite_ref-schmidhuber2015_244-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmidhuber2015-244">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
CNNs with 12 convolutional layers were used in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Reinforcement_learning" title="Reinforcement learning">reinforcement learning</a> by Deepmind's "<a href="/wiki/AlphaGo" title="AlphaGo">AlphaGo</a> Lee", the program that beat a top <a href="/wiki/Go_(game)" title="Go (game)">Go</a> champion in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature2017_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature2017-256">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deep_recurrent_neural_networks">Deep recurrent neural networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Deep recurrent neural networks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Recurrent neural networks">Recurrent neural networks</a></div>

Early on, deep learning was also applied to sequence learning with <a href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Recurrent neural networks">recurrent neural networks</a> (RNNs)<sup id="cite_ref-Recurrent_neural_networks_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Recurrent_neural_networks-257">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> which are in theory Turing complete<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> and can run arbitrary programs to process arbitrary sequences of inputs. The depth of an RNN is unlimited and depends on the length of its input sequence; thus, an RNN is an example of deep learning.<sup id="cite_ref-schmidhuber2015_244-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmidhuber2015-244">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> RNNs can be trained by <a href="/wiki/Gradient_descent" title="Gradient descent">gradient descent</a><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> but suffer from the <a href="/wiki/Vanishing_gradient_problem" title="Vanishing gradient problem">vanishing gradient problem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-goodfellow2016_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodfellow2016-245">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hochreiter1991_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hochreiter1991-262">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> In 1992, it was shown that unsupervised pre-training of a stack of <a href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_network" title="Recurrent neural network">recurrent neural networks</a> can speed up subsequent supervised learning of deep sequential problems.<sup id="cite_ref-SCHMID1992_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SCHMID1992-263">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Numerous researchers now use variants of a deep learning recurrent NN called the <a href="/wiki/Long_short-term_memory" title="Long short-term memory">long short-term memory</a> (LSTM) network published by Hochreiter &amp; Schmidhuber in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-lstm_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lstm-264">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> LSTM is often trained by Connectionist Temporal Classification (CTC).<sup id="cite_ref-graves2006_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graves2006-265">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> At Google, Microsoft and Baidu this approach has revolutionised <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">speech recognition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hannun2014_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hannun2014-266">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sak2014_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sak2014-267">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-liwu2015_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liwu2015-268">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> For example, in 2015, Google's speech recognition experienced a dramatic performance jump of 49% through CTC-trained LSTM, which is now available through <a href="/wiki/Google_Voice" title="Google Voice">Google Voice</a> to billions of smartphone users.<sup id="cite_ref-sak2015_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sak2015-269">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> Google also used LSTM to improve machine translation,<sup id="cite_ref-sutskever2014_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sutskever2014-270">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Language Modeling<sup id="cite_ref-vinyals2016_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vinyals2016-271">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> and Multilingual Language Processing.<sup id="cite_ref-gillick2015_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gillick2015-272">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> LSTM combined with CNNs also improved automatic image captioning<sup id="cite_ref-vinyals2015_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vinyals2015-273">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> and a plethora of other applications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evaluating_progress">Evaluating progress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Evaluating progress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Progress_in_artificial_intelligence" title="Progress in artificial intelligence">Progress in artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Competitions_and_prizes_in_artificial_intelligence" title="Competitions and prizes in artificial intelligence">Competitions and prizes in artificial intelligence</a></div>

AI, like electricity or the steam engine, is a general purpose technology.
