There is no consensus on how to characterize which tasks AI tends to excel at.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> While projects such as <a href="/wiki/AlphaZero" title="AlphaZero">AlphaZero</a> have succeeded in generating their own knowledge from scratch, many other machine learning projects require large training datasets.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> Researcher <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ng" title="Andrew Ng">Andrew Ng</a> has suggested, as a "highly imperfect rule of thumb", that "almost anything a typical human can do with less than one second of mental thought, we can probably now or in the near future automate using AI."<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moravec%27s_paradox" title="Moravec&#39;s paradox">Moravec's paradox</a> suggests that AI lags humans at many tasks that the human brain has specifically evolved to perform well.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Economist_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Economist-132">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Games provide a well-publicized benchmark for assessing rates of progress. <a href="/wiki/AlphaGo" title="AlphaGo">AlphaGo</a> around 2016 brought the era of classical board-game benchmarks to a close. Games of <a href="/wiki/Perfect_knowledge" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfect knowledge">imperfect knowledge</a> provide new challenges to AI in the area of <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/E-sports" class="mw-redirect" title="E-sports">E-sports</a> such as <a href="/wiki/StarCraft" title="StarCraft">StarCraft</a> continue to provide additional public benchmarks.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> There are many competitions and prizes, such as the <a href="/wiki/ImageNet_challenge" class="mw-redirect" title="ImageNet challenge">Imagenet Challenge</a>, to promote research in artificial intelligence. The most common areas of competition include general machine intelligence, conversational behavior, data-mining, <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_car" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous car">robotic cars</a>, and robot soccer as well as conventional games.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
The "imitation game" (an interpretation of the 1950 <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a> that assesses whether a computer can imitate a human) is nowadays considered too exploitable to be a meaningful benchmark.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> A derivative of the Turing test is the Completely Automated Public Turing test to tell Computers and Humans Apart (<a href="/wiki/CAPTCHA" title="CAPTCHA">CAPTCHA</a>). As the name implies, this helps to determine that a user is an actual person and not a computer posing as a human. In contrast to the standard Turing test, CAPTCHA is administered by a machine and targeted to a human as opposed to being administered by a human and targeted to a machine. A computer asks a user to complete a simple test then generates a grade for that test. Computers are unable to solve the problem, so correct solutions are deemed to be the result of a person taking the test. A common type of CAPTCHA is the test that requires the typing of distorted letters, numbers or symbols that appear in an image undecipherable by a computer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;BrienMarakas2011_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;BrienMarakas2011-284">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Proposed "universal intelligence" tests aim to compare how well machines, humans, and even non-human animals perform on problem sets that are generic as possible. At an extreme, the test suite can contain every possible problem, weighted by <a href="/wiki/Kolmogorov_complexity" title="Kolmogorov complexity">Kolmogorov complexity</a>; unfortunately, these problem sets tend to be dominated by impoverished pattern-matching exercises where a tuned AI can easily exceed human performance levels.<sup id="cite_ref-Mathematical_definitions_of_intelligence_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathematical_definitions_of_intelligence-285">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications<span id="Goals"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Automated_online_assistant.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Automated_online_assistant.png/220px-Automated_online_assistant.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Automated_online_assistant.png/330px-Automated_online_assistant.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8b/Automated_online_assistant.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="501" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Automated_online_assistant.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Automated_online_assistant" class="mw-redirect" title="Automated online assistant">automated online assistant</a> providing customer service on a web page – one of many very primitive applications of artificial intelligence</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Applications_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Applications of artificial intelligence">Applications of artificial intelligence</a></div>

AI is relevant to any intellectual task.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20091_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20091-287">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> Modern artificial intelligence techniques are pervasive and are too numerous to list here. Frequently, when a technique reaches mainstream use, it is no longer considered artificial intelligence; this phenomenon is described as the <a href="/wiki/AI_effect" title="AI effect">AI effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;CNN&#39;&#39;2006_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;CNN&#39;&#39;2006-288">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
High-profile examples of AI include autonomous vehicles (such as <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">drones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-driving_cars" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-driving cars">self-driving cars</a>), medical diagnosis, creating art (such as poetry), proving mathematical theorems, playing games (such as Chess or Go), search engines (such as <a href="/wiki/Google_search" class="mw-redirect" title="Google search">Google search</a>), online assistants (such as <a href="/wiki/Siri" title="Siri">Siri</a>), image recognition in photographs, spam filtering, predicting flight delays,<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> prediction of judicial decisions<sup id="cite_ref-ecthr2016_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecthr2016-290">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> and targeting online advertisements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20091_287-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20091-287">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
With social media sites overtaking TV as a source for news for young people and news organizations increasingly reliant on social media platforms for generating distribution,<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> major publishers now use artificial intelligence (AI) technology to post stories more effectively and generate higher volumes of traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Healthcare">Healthcare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Healthcare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_healthcare" title="Artificial intelligence in healthcare">Artificial intelligence in healthcare</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Laproscopic_Surgery_Robot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Laproscopic_Surgery_Robot.jpg/220px-Laproscopic_Surgery_Robot.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Laproscopic_Surgery_Robot.jpg/330px-Laproscopic_Surgery_Robot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Laproscopic_Surgery_Robot.jpg/440px-Laproscopic_Surgery_Robot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="891" data-file-height="1296" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Laproscopic_Surgery_Robot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A patient-side surgical arm of <a href="/wiki/Da_Vinci_Surgical_System" title="Da Vinci Surgical System">Da Vinci Surgical System</a></div></div></div>
AI is being applied to the high cost problem of dosage issues—where findings suggested that AI could save $16 billion. In 2016, a ground breaking study in California found that a mathematical formula developed with the help of AI correctly determined the accurate dose of immunosuppressant drugs to give to organ patients.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:X-ray_of_a_hand_with_automatic_bone_age_calculation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/X-ray_of_a_hand_with_automatic_bone_age_calculation.jpg/220px-X-ray_of_a_hand_with_automatic_bone_age_calculation.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/X-ray_of_a_hand_with_automatic_bone_age_calculation.jpg/330px-X-ray_of_a_hand_with_automatic_bone_age_calculation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/X-ray_of_a_hand_with_automatic_bone_age_calculation.jpg/440px-X-ray_of_a_hand_with_automatic_bone_age_calculation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1303" data-file-height="1827" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:X-ray_of_a_hand_with_automatic_bone_age_calculation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Projectional_radiography" title="Projectional radiography">X-ray</a> of a hand, with automatic calculation of <a href="/wiki/Bone_age" title="Bone age">bone age</a> by computer software</div></div></div>

Artificial intelligence is breaking into the healthcare industry by assisting doctors. According to Bloomberg Technology, Microsoft has developed AI to help doctors find the right treatments for cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> There is a great amount of research and drugs developed relating to cancer. In detail, there are more than 800 medicines and vaccines to treat cancer. This negatively affects the doctors, because there are too many options to choose from, making it more difficult to choose the right drugs for the patients. Microsoft is working on a project to develop a machine called "Hanover"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. Its goal is to memorize all the papers necessary to cancer and help predict which combinations of drugs will be most effective for each patient. One project that is being worked on at the moment is fighting <a href="/wiki/Acute_myeloid_leukemia" title="Acute myeloid leukemia">myeloid leukemia</a>, a fatal cancer where the treatment has not improved in decades. Another study was reported to have found that artificial intelligence was as good as trained doctors in identifying skin cancers.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> Another study is using artificial intelligence to try and monitor multiple high-risk patients, and this is done by asking each patient numerous questions based on data acquired from live doctor to patient interactions.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> One study was done with transfer learning, the machine performed a diagnosis similarly to a well-trained ophthalmologist, and could generate a decision within 30 seconds on whether or not the patient should be referred for treatment, with more than 95% accuracy.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
According to <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>, a recent study by surgeons at the Children's National Medical Center in Washington successfully demonstrated surgery with an autonomous robot. The team supervised the robot while it performed soft-tissue surgery, stitching together a pig's bowel during open surgery, and doing so better than a human surgeon, the team claimed.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> IBM has created its own artificial intelligence computer, the <a href="/wiki/IBM_Watson" class="mw-redirect" title="IBM Watson">IBM Watson</a>, which has beaten human intelligence (at some levels).  Watson not only won at the game show <i>Jeopardy!</i> against former champions,<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> but was declared a hero after successfully diagnosing a woman who was suffering from leukemia.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Automotive">Automotive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Automotive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Driverless_cars" class="mw-redirect" title="Driverless cars">driverless cars</a></div>

Advancements in AI have contributed to the growth of the automotive industry through the creation and evolution of self-driving vehicles. As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, there are over 30 companies utilizing AI into the creation of <a href="/wiki/Driverless_cars" class="mw-redirect" title="Driverless cars">driverless cars</a>. A few companies involved with AI include <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" class="mw-redirect" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Many components contribute to the functioning of self-driving cars. These vehicles incorporate systems such as braking, lane changing, collision prevention, navigation and mapping. Together, these systems, as well as high performance computers, are integrated into one complex vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Recent developments in autonomous automobiles have made the innovation of self-driving trucks possible, though they are still in the testing phase. The UK government has passed legislation to begin testing of self-driving truck platoons in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> Self-driving truck platoons are a fleet of self-driving trucks following the lead of one non-self-driving truck, so the truck platoons aren't entirely autonomous yet. Meanwhile, the Daimler, a German automobile corporation, is testing the Freightliner Inspiration which is a semi-autonomous truck that will only be used on the highway.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
One main factor that influences the ability for a driver-less automobile to function is mapping. In general, the vehicle would be pre-programmed with a map of the area being driven. This map would include data on the approximations of street light and curb heights in order for the vehicle to be aware of its surroundings. However, Google has been working on an algorithm with the purpose of eliminating the need for pre-programmed maps and instead, creating a device that would be able to adjust to a variety of new surroundings.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> Some self-driving cars are not equipped with steering wheels or brake pedals, so there has also been research focused on creating an algorithm that is capable of maintaining a safe environment for the passengers in the vehicle through awareness of speed and driving conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Another factor that is influencing the ability for a driver-less automobile is the safety of the passenger.  To make a driver-less automobile, engineers must program it to handle high-risk situations. These situations could include a head-on collision with pedestrians.  The car's main goal should be to make a decision that would avoid hitting the pedestrians and saving the passengers in the car.  But there is a possibility the car would need to make a decision that would put someone in danger. In other words, the car would need to decide to save the pedestrians or the passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> The programming of the car in these situations is crucial to a successful driver-less automobile.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finance_and_economics">Finance and economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Finance and economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<a href="/wiki/Financial_institution" title="Financial institution">Financial institutions</a> have long used <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural network</a> systems to detect charges or claims outside of the norm, flagging these for human investigation. The use of AI in <a href="/wiki/Banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Banking">banking</a> can be traced back to 1987 when <a href="/wiki/Security_Pacific_National_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Security Pacific National Bank">Security Pacific National Bank</a> in US set-up a Fraud Prevention Task force to counter the unauthorized use of debit cards<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. Programs like Kasisto and Moneystream are using AI in financial services.
</p>
Banks use artificial intelligence systems today to organize operations, maintain book-keeping, invest in stocks, and manage properties. AI can react to changes overnight or when business is not taking place.<sup id="cite_ref-Eleanor_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eleanor-310">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> In August 2001, robots beat humans in a simulated <a href="/wiki/Stock_trader" title="Stock trader">financial trading</a> competition.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> AI has also reduced fraud and financial crimes by monitoring <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_pattern" title="Behavioral pattern">behavioral patterns</a> of users for any abnormal changes or anomalies.<sup id="cite_ref-fsroundtable.org_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fsroundtable.org-312">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
The use of AI machines in the market in applications such as online trading and decision making has changed major economic theories.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> For example, AI based buying and selling platforms have changed the law of <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a> in that it is now possible to easily estimate individualized demand and supply curves and thus individualized pricing. Furthermore, AI machines reduce <a href="/wiki/Information_asymmetry" title="Information asymmetry">information asymmetry</a> in the market and thus making markets more efficient while reducing the volume of trades<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. Furthermore, AI in the markets limits the consequences of behavior in the markets again making markets more efficient<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. Other theories where AI has had impact include in <a href="/wiki/Rational_choice" class="mw-redirect" title="Rational choice">rational choice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rational_expectations" title="Rational expectations">rational expectations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_turning_point" title="Lewis turning point">Lewis turning point</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portfolio_optimization" title="Portfolio optimization">portfolio optimization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Counterfactual_thinking" title="Counterfactual thinking">counterfactual thinking</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_government" title="Artificial intelligence in government">Artificial intelligence in government</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_China" title="Mass surveillance in China">Mass surveillance in China</a></div>

Artificial intelligence paired with <a href="/wiki/Facial_recognition_system" title="Facial recognition system">facial recognition systems</a> may be used for mass surveillance. This is already the case in some parts of China.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Video_games">Video games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Video games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_(video_games)" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial intelligence (video games)">Artificial intelligence (video games)</a></div>

In video games, artificial intelligence is routinely used to generate dynamic purposeful behavior in <a href="/wiki/Non-player_character" title="Non-player character">non-player characters</a> (NPCs). In addition, well-understood AI techniques are routinely used for <a href="/wiki/Pathfinding" title="Pathfinding">pathfinding</a>. Some researchers consider NPC AI in games to be a "solved problem" for most production tasks. Games with more atypical AI include the AI director of <i><a href="/wiki/Left_4_Dead" title="Left 4 Dead">Left 4 Dead</a></i> (2008) and the neuroevolutionary training of platoons in <i><a href="/wiki/Supreme_Commander_2" title="Supreme Commander 2">Supreme Commander 2</a></i> (2010).<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_arms_race" title="Artificial intelligence arms race">Artificial intelligence arms race</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lethal_autonomous_weapon" title="Lethal autonomous weapon">Lethal autonomous weapon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_combat_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned combat aerial vehicle">Unmanned combat aerial vehicle</a></div>

The main military applications of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning are to enhance C2, Communications, Sensors, Integration and Interoperability<sup id="cite_ref-AI_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI-318">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>.
Artifical Intelligence technologies enables coordination  of  sensors and effectors, threat detection and identification, marking of enemy positions, target acquisition, coordination and deconfliction of distributed Join Fires between networked combat vehicles and tanks also  inside  Manned  and  Unmanned Teams (MUM-T)<sup id="cite_ref-AI_318-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI-318">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>.
</p>
Worldwide annual military spending on robotics rose from US$5.1 billion in 2010 to US$7.5 billion in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Military drones capable of autonomous action are widely considered a useful asset.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>  Many artificial intelligence researchers seek to distance themselves from military applications of AI.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Audit">Audit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Audit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

For financial statements audit, AI makes continuous audit possible. AI tools could analyze many sets of different information immediately. The potential benefit would be the overall audit risk will be reduced, the level of assurance will be increased and the time duration of audit will be reduced.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising">Advertising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Advertising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

It is possible to use AI to predict or generalize the behavior of customers from their <a href="/wiki/Digital_footprints" class="mw-redirect" title="Digital footprints">digital footprints</a> in order to target them with personalized promotions or build customer personas automatically.<sup id="cite_ref-Matz_et_al_2017_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matz_et_al_2017-324">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> A documented case reports that online gambling companies were using AI to improve customer targeting.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
Moreover, the application of <a href="/wiki/Personality_computing" title="Personality computing">Personality computing</a> AI models can help reducing the cost of advertising campaigns by adding psychological targeting to more traditional sociodemographic or behavioral targeting.<sup id="cite_ref-Celli_et_al._2017_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Celli_et_al._2017-326">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Computer_art" title="Computer art">Computer art</a></div>

Artificial Intelligence has inspired numerous creative applications including its usage to produce visual art. The exhibition "Thinking Machines: Art and Design in the Computer Age, 1959–1989" at MoMA<sup id="cite_ref-moma_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moma-327">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> provides a good overview of the historical applications of AI for art, architecture, and design. Recent exhibitions showcasing the usage of AI to produce art include the Google-sponsored benefit and auction at the Gray Area Foundation in San Francisco, where artists experimented with the deepdream algorithm<sup id="cite_ref-wp1_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wp1-328">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> and the exhibition "Unhuman: Art in the Age of AI," which took place in Los Angeles and Frankfurt in the fall of 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-sf_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sf-329">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-artsy_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artsy-330">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> In the spring of 2018, the Association of Computing Machinery dedicated a special magazine issue to the subject of computers and art highlighting the role of machine learning in the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-acm_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acm-331">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_and_ethics">Philosophy and ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Philosophy and ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Philosophy of artificial intelligence">Philosophy of artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Ethics of artificial intelligence">Ethics of artificial intelligence</a></div>

There are three philosophical questions related to AI:
</p>
<ol><li>Is <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">artificial general intelligence</a> possible? Can a machine solve any problem that a human being can solve using intelligence? Or are there hard limits to what a machine can accomplish?</li>
<li>Are intelligent machines dangerous? How can we ensure that machines behave ethically and that they are used ethically?</li>
<li>Can a machine have a <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">mental states</a> in exactly the same sense that human beings do? Can a machine be <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentient</a>, and thus deserve certain rights? Can a machine <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intentionally</a> cause harm?</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_limits_of_artificial_general_intelligence">The limits of artificial general intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: The limits of artificial general intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophy of AI">Philosophy of AI</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_symbol_systems_hypothesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical symbol systems hypothesis">Physical symbol systems hypothesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dreyfus%27_critique_of_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreyfus&#39; critique of AI">Dreyfus' critique of AI</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_New_Mind" title="The Emperor&#39;s New Mind">The Emperor's New Mind</a>, and <a href="/wiki/AI_effect" title="AI effect">AI effect</a></div>

Can a machine be intelligent? Can it "think"?
</p>
<dl><dt><i><a href="/wiki/Computing_Machinery_and_Intelligence" title="Computing Machinery and Intelligence">Alan Turing's "polite convention"</a></i></dt>
<dd>We need not decide if a machine can "think"; we need only decide if a machine can act as intelligently as a human being. This approach to the philosophical problems associated with artificial intelligence forms the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turing_test_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turing_test-332">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt><i>The <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Workshop" class="mw-redirect" title="Dartmouth Workshop">Dartmouth proposal</a></i></dt>
<dd>"Every aspect of learning or any other feature of intelligence can be so precisely described that a machine can be made to simulate it." This conjecture was printed in the proposal for the Dartmouth Conference of 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_proposal_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_proposal-333">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt><i><a href="/wiki/Physical_symbol_system" title="Physical symbol system">Newell and Simon's physical symbol system hypothesis</a></i></dt>
<dd>"A physical symbol system has the necessary and sufficient means of general intelligent action." Newell and Simon argue that intelligence consists of formal operations on symbols.<sup id="cite_ref-Physical_symbol_system_hypothesis_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Physical_symbol_system_hypothesis-334">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus" title="Hubert Dreyfus">Hubert Dreyfus</a> argued that, on the contrary, human expertise depends on unconscious instinct rather than conscious symbol manipulation and on having a "feel" for the situation rather than explicit symbolic knowledge. (See <a href="/wiki/Dreyfus%27_critique_of_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreyfus&#39; critique of AI">Dreyfus' critique of AI</a>.)<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dreyfus&#39;_critique_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dreyfus&#39;_critique-336">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt><i>Gödelian arguments</i></dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del" class="mw-redirect" title="Gödel">Gödel</a> himself,<sup id="cite_ref-Gödel_himself_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gödel_himself-337">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Lucas_(philosopher)" title="John Lucas (philosopher)">John Lucas</a> (in 1961) and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose" title="Roger Penrose">Roger Penrose</a> (in a more detailed argument from 1989 onwards) made highly technical arguments that human mathematicians can consistently see the truth of their own "Gödel statements" and therefore have computational abilities beyond that of mechanical Turing machines.<sup id="cite_ref-The_mathematical_objection_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_mathematical_objection-338">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> However, some people do not agree with the "Gödelian arguments". <sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt><i>The <a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">artificial brain</a> argument</i></dt>
<dd>The brain can be simulated by machines and because brains are intelligent, simulated brains must also be intelligent; thus machines can be intelligent. <a href="/wiki/Hans_Moravec" title="Hans Moravec">Hans Moravec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> and others have argued that it is technologically feasible to copy the brain directly into hardware and software and that such a simulation will be essentially identical to the original.<sup id="cite_ref-Brain_simulation_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brain_simulation-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt><i>The <a href="/wiki/AI_effect" title="AI effect">AI effect</a></i></dt>
<dd>Machines are <i>already</i> intelligent, but observers have failed to recognize it. When <a href="/wiki/Deep_Blue_(chess_computer)" title="Deep Blue (chess computer)">Deep Blue</a> beat <a href="/wiki/Garry_Kasparov" title="Garry Kasparov">Garry Kasparov</a> in chess, the machine was acting intelligently. However, onlookers commonly discount the behavior of an artificial intelligence program by arguing that it is not "real" intelligence after all; thus "real" intelligence is whatever intelligent behavior people can do that machines still cannot. This is known as the AI Effect: "AI is whatever hasn't been done yet."</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_harm">Potential harm<span id="Potential_risks_and_moral_reasoning"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Potential harm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

Widespread use of artificial intelligence could have <a href="/wiki/Unintended_consequences" title="Unintended consequences">unintended consequences</a> that are dangerous or undesirable. Scientists from the <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Life_Institute" title="Future of Life Institute">Future of Life Institute</a>, among others, described some short-term research goals to see how AI influences the economy, the laws and ethics that are involved with AI and how to minimize AI security risks. In the long-term, the scientists have proposed to continue optimizing function while minimizing possible security risks that come along with new technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
The potential negative effects of AI and automation are a major issue for <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Yang" title="Andrew Yang">Andrew Yang</a>'s presidential campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Existential_risk">Existential risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Existential risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Existential_risk_from_artificial_general_intelligence" title="Existential risk from artificial general intelligence">Existential risk from artificial general intelligence</a></div>

Physicist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> have expressed concerns about the possibility that AI could evolve to the point that humans could not control it, with Hawking theorizing that this could "<a href="/wiki/Global_catastrophic_risk" title="Global catastrophic risk">spell the end of the human race</a>".
<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup>
<sup id="cite_ref-Holley_345-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holley-345">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup>
<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote">
The development of full artificial intelligence could spell the end of the human race. Once humans develop artificial intelligence, it will take off on its own and redesign itself at an ever-increasing rate. Humans, who are limited by slow biological evolution, couldn't compete and would be superseded.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a><sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>

In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Superintelligence:_Paths,_Dangers,_Strategies" title="Superintelligence: Paths, Dangers, Strategies">Superintelligence</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Bostrom" title="Nick Bostrom">Nick Bostrom</a> provides an argument that artificial intelligence will pose a threat to humankind. He argues that sufficiently intelligent AI, if it chooses actions based on achieving some goal, will exhibit <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_convergence" title="Instrumental convergence">convergent</a> behavior such as acquiring resources or protecting itself from being shut down. If this AI's goals do not reflect humanity's—one example is an AI told to compute as many digits of pi as possible—it might harm humanity in order to acquire more resources or prevent itself from being shut down, ultimately to better achieve its goal.
</p>
Concern over risk from artificial intelligence has led to some high-profile donations and investments. A group of prominent tech titans including <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thiel" title="Peter Thiel">Peter Thiel</a>, Amazon Web Services and Musk have committed $1billion to <a href="/wiki/OpenAI" title="OpenAI">OpenAI</a>, a nonprofit company aimed at championing responsible AI development.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> The opinion of experts within the field of artificial intelligence is mixed, with sizable fractions both concerned and unconcerned by risk from eventual superhumanly-capable AI.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup> Other technology industry leaders believe that artificial intelligence is helpful in its current form and will continue to assist humans. Oracle CEO <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hurd" title="Mark Hurd">Mark Hurd</a> has stated that AI "will actually create more jobs, not less jobs" as humans will be needed to manage AI systems.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> Facebook CEO <a href="/wiki/Mark_Zuckerberg" title="Mark Zuckerberg">Mark Zuckerberg</a> believes AI will "unlock a huge amount of positive things," such as curing disease and increasing the safety of autonomous cars.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
In January 2015, <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> donated ten million dollars to the <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Life_Institute" title="Future of Life Institute">Future of Life Institute</a> to fund research on understanding AI decision making. The goal of the institute is to "grow wisdom with which we manage" the growing power of technology. Musk also funds companies developing artificial intelligence such as <a href="/wiki/Google_DeepMind" class="mw-redirect" title="Google DeepMind">Google DeepMind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vicarious_(company)" title="Vicarious (company)">Vicarious</a> to "just keep an eye on what's going on with artificial intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup> I think there is potentially a dangerous outcome there."<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
For this danger to be realized, the hypothetical AI would have to overpower or out-think all of humanity, which a minority of experts argue is a possibility far enough in the future to not be worth researching.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> Other counterarguments revolve around humans being either intrinsically or convergently valuable from the perspective of an artificial intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Devaluation_of_humanity">Devaluation of humanity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Devaluation of humanity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Computer_Power_and_Human_Reason" title="Computer Power and Human Reason">Computer Power and Human Reason</a></div>

<a href="/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum" title="Joseph Weizenbaum">Joseph Weizenbaum</a> wrote that AI applications cannot, by definition, successfully simulate genuine human empathy and that the use of AI technology in fields such as <a href="/wiki/Customer_service" title="Customer service">customer service</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapy</a><sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> was deeply misguided. Weizenbaum was also bothered that AI researchers (and some philosophers) were willing to view the human mind as nothing more than a computer program (a position is now known as <a href="/wiki/Computationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Computationalism">computationalism</a>). To Weizenbaum these points suggest that AI research devalues human life.<sup id="cite_ref-Weizenbaum&#39;s_critique_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weizenbaum&#39;s_critique-359">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_justice">Social justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Social justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

One concern is that AI programs may be programmed to be biased against certain groups, such as women and minorities, because most of the developers are wealthy Caucasian men.<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> Support for artificial intelligence is higher among men (with 47% approving) than women (35% approving).
</p>
Algorithms have a host of applications in today’s legal system already, assisting officials ranging from judges to parole officers and public defenders in gauging the predicted likelihood of recidivism of defendants.<sup id="cite_ref-propublica.org_361-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-propublica.org-361">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> COMPAS (an acronym for Correctional Offender Management Profiling for Alternative Sanctions) counts among the most widely utilized commercially available solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-propublica.org_361-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-propublica.org-361">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> It has been suggested that COMPAS assigns an exceptionally elevated risk of recidivism to black defendants while, conversely, ascribing low risk estimate to white defendants significantly more often than statistically expected. <sup id="cite_ref-propublica.org_361-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-propublica.org-361">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decrease_in_demand_for_human_labor">Decrease in demand for human labor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Decrease in demand for human labor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Technological_unemployment#21st_century" title="Technological unemployment">Technological unemployment §&#160;21st century</a></div>

The relationship between automation and employment is complicated. While automation eliminates old jobs, it also creates new jobs through micro-economic and macro-economic effects.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> Unlike previous waves of automation, many middle-class jobs may be eliminated by artificial intelligence; <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> states that "the worry that AI could do to white-collar jobs what steam power did to blue-collar ones during the Industrial Revolution" is "worth taking seriously".<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> Subjective estimates of the risk vary widely; for example, Michael Osborne and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benedikt_Frey" title="Carl Benedikt Frey">Carl Benedikt Frey</a> estimate 47% of U.S. jobs are at "high risk" of potential automation, while an OECD report classifies only 9% of U.S. jobs as "high risk".<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> Jobs at extreme risk range from paralegals to fast food cooks, while job demand is likely to increase for care-related professions ranging from personal healthcare to the clergy.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Author <a href="/wiki/Martin_Ford_(author)" title="Martin Ford (author)">Martin Ford</a> and others go further and argue that a large number of jobs are routine, repetitive and (to an AI) predictable; Ford warns that these jobs may be automated in the next couple of decades, and that many of the new jobs may not be "accessible to people with average capability", even with retraining. Economists point out that in the past technology has tended to increase rather than reduce total employment, but acknowledge that "we're in uncharted territory" with AI.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_jobs_debate_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_jobs_debate-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Autonomous_weapons">Autonomous weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Autonomous weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lethal_autonomous_weapon" title="Lethal autonomous weapon">Lethal autonomous weapon</a></div>

Currently, 50+ countries are researching battlefield robots, including the United States, China, Russia, and the United Kingdom. Many people concerned about risk from superintelligent AI also want to limit the use of artificial soldiers and drones.<sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethical_machines">Ethical machines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Ethical machines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

Machines with intelligence have the potential to use their intelligence to prevent harm and minimize the risks; they may have the ability to use <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical reasoning</a> to better choose their actions in the world. Research in this area includes <a href="/wiki/Machine_ethics" title="Machine ethics">machine ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_moral_agents" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial moral agents">artificial moral agents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Friendly_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Friendly AI">friendly AI</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial_moral_agents">Artificial moral agents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Artificial moral agents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

Wendell Wallach introduced the concept of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_moral_agents" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial moral agents">artificial moral agents</a> (AMA) in his book <i>Moral Machines</i><sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> For Wallach, AMAs have become a part of the research landscape of artificial intelligence as guided by its two central questions which he identifies as "Does Humanity Want Computers Making Moral Decisions"<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup> and "Can (Ro)bots Really Be Moral".<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> For Wallach the question is not centered on the issue of <i>whether</i> machines can demonstrate the equivalent of moral behavior in contrast to the <i>constraints</i> which society may place on the development of AMAs.<sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Machine_ethics">Machine ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Machine ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Machine_ethics" title="Machine ethics">Machine ethics</a></div>

The field of machine ethics is concerned with giving machines ethical principles, or a procedure for discovering a way to resolve the ethical dilemmas they might encounter, enabling them to function in an ethically responsible manner through their own ethical decision making.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_373-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-373">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> The field was delineated in the AAAI Fall 2005 Symposium on Machine Ethics: "Past research concerning the relationship between technology and ethics has largely focused on responsible and irresponsible use of technology by human beings, with a few people being interested in how human beings ought to treat machines. In all cases, only human beings have engaged in ethical reasoning. The time has come for adding an ethical dimension to at least some machines. Recognition of the ethical ramifications of behavior involving machines, as well as recent and potential developments in machine autonomy, necessitate this. In contrast to computer hacking, software property issues, privacy issues and other topics normally ascribed to computer ethics, machine ethics is concerned with the behavior of machines towards human users and other machines. Research in machine ethics is key to alleviating concerns with autonomous systems—it could be argued that the notion of autonomous machines without such a dimension is at the root of all fear concerning machine intelligence. Further, investigation of machine ethics could enable the discovery of problems with current ethical theories, advancing our thinking about Ethics."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_374-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-374">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup> Machine ethics is sometimes referred to as machine morality, computational ethics or computational morality. A variety of perspectives of this nascent field can be found in the collected edition "Machine Ethics"<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_373-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-373">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> that stems from the AAAI Fall 2005 Symposium on Machine Ethics.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_374-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-374">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malevolent_and_friendly_AI">Malevolent and friendly AI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Malevolent and friendly AI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Friendly_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Friendly AI">Friendly AI</a></div>

Political scientist <a href="/wiki/Charles_T._Rubin" title="Charles T. Rubin">Charles T. Rubin</a> believes that AI can be neither designed nor guaranteed to be benevolent.<sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> He argues that "any sufficiently advanced benevolence may be indistinguishable from malevolence." Humans should not assume machines or robots would treat us favorably because there is no <i>a priori</i> reason to believe that they would be sympathetic to our system of morality, which has evolved along with our particular biology (which AIs would not share). Hyper-intelligent software may not necessarily decide to support the continued existence of humanity and would be extremely difficult to stop. This topic has also recently begun to be discussed in academic publications as a real source of risks to civilization, humans, and planet Earth.
</p>
One proposal to deal with this is to ensure that the first generally intelligent AI is '<a href="/wiki/Friendly_AI" class="mw-redirect" title="Friendly AI">Friendly AI</a>' and will be able to control subsequently developed AIs. Some question whether this kind of check could actually remain in place.
</p>
Leading AI researcher <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Brooks" title="Rodney Brooks">Rodney Brooks</a> writes, "I think it is a mistake to be worrying about us developing malevolent AI anytime in the next few hundred years. I think the worry stems from a fundamental error in not distinguishing the difference between the very real recent advances in a particular aspect of AI, and the enormity and complexity of building sentient volitional intelligence."<sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Machine_consciousness.2C_sentience_and_mind"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Machine_consciousness,_sentience_and_mind">Machine consciousness, sentience and mind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Machine consciousness, sentience and mind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_consciousness" title="Artificial consciousness">Artificial consciousness</a></div>

If an AI system replicates all key aspects of human intelligence, will that system also be <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentient</a>—will it have a <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> which has <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">conscious experiences</a>? This question is closely related to the philosophical problem as to the nature of human consciousness, generally referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Hard_problem_of_consciousness" title="Hard problem of consciousness">hard problem of consciousness</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consciousness">Consciousness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Consciousness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hard_problem_of_consciousness" title="Hard problem of consciousness">Hard problem of consciousness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_mind" title="Theory of mind">Theory of mind</a></div>

<a href="/wiki/David_Chalmers" title="David Chalmers">David Chalmers</a> identified two problems in understanding the mind, which he named the "hard" and "easy" problems of consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-Chalmers_377-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-377">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> The easy problem is understanding how the brain processes signals, makes plans and controls behavior. The hard problem is explaining how this <i>feels</i> or why it should feel like anything at all. Human <a href="/wiki/Information_processing" title="Information processing">information processing</a> is easy to explain, however human <a href="/wiki/Subjective_experience" class="mw-redirect" title="Subjective experience">subjective experience</a> is difficult to explain.
</p>
For example, consider what happens when a person is shown a color swatch and identifies it, saying "it's red". The easy problem only requires understanding the machinery in the brain that makes it possible for a person to know that the color swatch is red. The hard problem is that people also know something else—they also know <i>what red looks like</i>. (Consider that a person born blind can know that something is red without knowing what red looks like.)<sup id="cite_ref-378" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-378">&#91;l&#93;</a></sup> Everyone knows subjective experience exists, because they do it every day (e.g., all sighted people know what red looks like). The hard problem is explaining how the brain creates it, why it exists, and how it is different from knowledge and other aspects of the brain.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Computationalism_and_functionalism">Computationalism and functionalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Computationalism and functionalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Computationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Computationalism">Computationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Functionalism (philosophy of mind)">Functionalism (philosophy of mind)</a></div>

Computationalism is the position in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a> that the human mind or the human brain (or both) is an information processing system and that thinking is a form of computing.<sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> Computationalism argues that the relationship between mind and body is similar or identical to the relationship between software and hardware and thus may be a solution to the <a href="/wiki/Mind-body_problem" class="mw-redirect" title="Mind-body problem">mind-body problem</a>. This philosophical position was inspired by the work of AI researchers and cognitive scientists in the 1960s and was originally proposed by philosophers <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Fodor" title="Jerry Fodor">Jerry Fodor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Putnam" title="Hilary Putnam">Hilary Putnam</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_AI_hypothesis">Strong AI hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Strong AI hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_room" title="Chinese room">Chinese room</a></div>

The philosophical position that <a href="/wiki/John_Searle" title="John Searle">John Searle</a> has named <a href="/wiki/Strong_AI_hypothesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Strong AI hypothesis">"strong AI"</a> states: "The appropriately programmed computer with the right inputs and outputs would thereby have a mind in exactly the same sense human beings have minds."<sup id="cite_ref-Searle&#39;s_strong_AI_380-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Searle&#39;s_strong_AI-380">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> Searle counters this assertion with his <a href="/wiki/Chinese_room" title="Chinese room">Chinese room</a> argument, which asks us to look <i>inside</i> the computer and try to find where the "mind" might be.<sup id="cite_ref-Chinese_room_381-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chinese_room-381">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Robot_rights">Robot rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Robot rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Robot_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Robot rights">Robot rights</a></div>

If a machine can be created that has intelligence, could it also <i><a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">feel</a></i>? If it can feel, does it have the same rights as a human? This issue, now known as "<a href="/wiki/Robot_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Robot rights">robot rights</a>", is currently being considered by, for example, California's <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_the_Future" title="Institute for the Future">Institute for the Future</a>, although many critics believe that the discussion is premature.<sup id="cite_ref-Robot_rights_382-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robot_rights-382">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> Some critics of <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">transhumanism</a> argue that any hypothetical robot rights would lie on a spectrum with <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animal rights</a> and human rights. <sup id="cite_ref-Evans_2015_383-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans_2015-383">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> The subject is profoundly discussed in the 2010 documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Plug_%26_Pray" title="Plug &amp; Pray">Plug &amp; Pray</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-384" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-384">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup> and many sci fi media such as <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">Star Trek</a> Next Generation, with the character of <a href="/wiki/Commander_Data" class="mw-redirect" title="Commander Data">Commander Data</a>, who fought being disassembled for research, and wanted to "become human", and the robotic holograms in Voyager.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Superintelligence">Superintelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Superintelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Superintelligence" title="Superintelligence">Superintelligence</a></div>

Are there limits to how intelligent machines—or human-machine hybrids—can be? A superintelligence, hyperintelligence, or superhuman intelligence is a hypothetical agent that would possess intelligence far surpassing that of the brightest and most gifted human mind. <i>Superintelligence</i> may also refer to the form or degree of intelligence possessed by such an agent.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Technological_singularity">Technological singularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Technological singularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">Technological singularity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore&#39;s law">Moore's law</a></div>

If research into <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">Strong AI</a> produced sufficiently intelligent software, it might be able to reprogram and improve itself. The improved software would be even better at improving itself, leading to <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_explosion" class="mw-redirect" title="Intelligence explosion">recursive self-improvement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-recurse_385-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-recurse-385">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup> The new intelligence could thus increase exponentially and dramatically surpass humans. Science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">Vernor Vinge</a> named this scenario "<a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">singularity</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Singularity_386-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singularity-386">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup> Technological singularity is when accelerating progress in technologies will cause a runaway effect wherein artificial intelligence will exceed human intellectual capacity and control, thus radically changing or even ending civilization. Because the capabilities of such an intelligence may be impossible to comprehend, the technological singularity is an occurrence beyond which events are unpredictable or even unfathomable.<sup id="cite_ref-Singularity_386-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singularity-386">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> has used <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore&#39;s law">Moore's law</a> (which describes the relentless exponential improvement in digital technology) to calculate that <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">desktop computers</a> will have the same processing power as human brains by the year 2029, and predicts that the singularity will occur in 2045.<sup id="cite_ref-Singularity_386-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singularity-386">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transhumanism">Transhumanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Transhumanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">Transhumanism</a></div>

Robot designer <a href="/wiki/Hans_Moravec" title="Hans Moravec">Hans Moravec</a>, cyberneticist <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Warwick" title="Kevin Warwick">Kevin Warwick</a> and inventor <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> have predicted that humans and machines will merge in the future into <a href="/wiki/Cyborg" title="Cyborg">cyborgs</a> that are more capable and powerful than either.<sup id="cite_ref-Transhumanism_387-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Transhumanism-387">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup> This idea, called <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">transhumanism</a>, which has roots in <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ettinger" title="Robert Ettinger">Robert Ettinger</a>.
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Edward_Fredkin" title="Edward Fredkin">Edward Fredkin</a> argues that "artificial intelligence is the next stage in evolution", an idea first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Butler_(novelist)" title="Samuel Butler (novelist)">Samuel Butler</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Darwin_among_the_Machines" title="Darwin among the Machines">Darwin among the Machines</a>" as far back as 1863, and expanded upon by <a href="/wiki/George_Dyson_(science_historian)" title="George Dyson (science historian)">George Dyson</a> in his book of the same name in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_as_evolution_388-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_as_evolution-388">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

The long-term economic effects of AI are uncertain. A survey of economists showed disagreement about whether the increasing use of robots and AI will cause a substantial increase in long-term <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a>, but they generally agree that it could be a net benefit, if <a href="/wiki/Productivity" title="Productivity">productivity</a> gains are <a href="/wiki/Redistribution_of_income_and_wealth" title="Redistribution of income and wealth">redistributed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-389" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-389">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction">In fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: In fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_fiction" title="Artificial intelligence in fiction">Artificial intelligence in fiction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Capek_play.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Capek_play.jpg/220px-Capek_play.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Capek_play.jpg/330px-Capek_play.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Capek_play.jpg/440px-Capek_play.jpg 2x" data-file-width="566" data-file-height="316" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Capek_play.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The word "robot" itself was coined by <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel Čapek">Karel Čapek</a> in his 1921 play <i><a href="/wiki/R.U.R." title="R.U.R.">R.U.R.</a></i>, the title standing for "<a href="/wiki/Rossum%27s_Universal_Robots" class="mw-redirect" title="Rossum&#39;s Universal Robots">Rossum's Universal Robots</a>"</div></div></div>

Thought-capable artificial beings appeared as storytelling devices since antiquity,<sup id="cite_ref-AI_in_myth_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_myth-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
and have been a persistent theme in <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>.
</p>
A common <a href="/wiki/Trope_(literature)" title="Trope (literature)">trope</a> in these works began with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i>, where a human creation becomes a threat to its masters. This includes such works as <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(novel)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (novel)">Arthur C.
