Clarke's</a> and <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (film)">Stanley Kubrick's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey" title="2001: A Space Odyssey">2001: A Space Odyssey</a></i> (both 1968), with <a href="/wiki/HAL_9000" title="HAL 9000">HAL 9000</a>, the murderous computer in charge of the <i><a href="/wiki/Discovery_One" title="Discovery One">Discovery One</a></i> spaceship, as well as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Terminator" title="The Terminator">The Terminator</a></i> (1984) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Matrix" title="The Matrix">The Matrix</a></i> (1999). In contrast, the rare loyal robots such as Gort from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Day_the_Earth_Stood_Still" title="The Day the Earth Stood Still">The Day the Earth Stood Still</a></i> (1951) and Bishop from <i><a href="/wiki/Aliens_(film)" title="Aliens (film)">Aliens</a></i> (1986) are less prominent in popular culture.<sup id="cite_ref-390" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-390">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Three_Laws_of_Robotics" title="Three Laws of Robotics">Three Laws of Robotics</a> in many books and stories, most notably the "Multivac" series about a super-intelligent computer of the same name. Asimov's laws are often brought up during lay discussions of machine ethics;<sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup> while almost all artificial intelligence researchers are familiar with Asimov's laws through popular culture, they generally consider the laws useless for many reasons, one of which is their ambiguity.<sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">Transhumanism</a> (the merging of humans and machines) is explored in the <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ghost_in_the_Shell" title="Ghost in the Shell">Ghost in the Shell</a></i> and the science-fiction series <i><a href="/wiki/Dune_(novel)" title="Dune (novel)">Dune</a></i>. In the 1980s, artist <a href="/wiki/Hajime_Sorayama" title="Hajime Sorayama">Hajime Sorayama</a>'s Sexy Robots series were painted and published in Japan depicting the actual organic human form with lifelike muscular metallic skins and later "the Gynoids" book followed that was used by or influenced movie makers including <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">George Lucas</a> and other creatives. Sorayama never considered these organic robots to be real part of nature but always unnatural product of the human mind, a fantasy existing in the mind even when realized in actual form.
</p>
Several works use AI to force us to confront the fundamental of question of what makes us human, showing us artificial beings that have <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">the ability to feel</a>, and thus to suffer. This appears in <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel Čapek">Karel Čapek</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/R.U.R." title="R.U.R.">R.U.R.</a>", the films "<a href="/wiki/A.I._Artificial_Intelligence" title="A.I. Artificial Intelligence">A.I. Artificial Intelligence</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Ex_Machina_(film)" title="Ex Machina (film)">Ex Machina</a>",  as well as the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Do_Androids_Dream_of_Electric_Sheep%3F" title="Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?">Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a>. Dick considers the idea that our understanding of human subjectivity is altered by technology created with artificial intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Fragmentation_of_AI-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Fragmentation_of_AI_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Fragmentation_of_AI_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">

<li id="cite_note-Problems_of_AI-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_14-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_14-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_14-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
This list of intelligent traits is based on the topics covered by the major AI textbooks, including:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a></li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFLugerStubblefield2004">Luger &amp; Stubblefield 2004</a></li>

<li id="cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_15-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_15-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_15-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
Biological intelligence vs. intelligence in general:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp.&#160;2–3, who make the analogy with <a href="/wiki/Aeronautical_engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Aeronautical engineering">aeronautical engineering</a>.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;100–101, who writes that there are "two major branches of artificial intelligence: one aimed at producing intelligent behavior regardless of how it was accomplished, and the other aimed at modeling intelligent processes found in nature, particularly human ones."</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFKolata1982">Kolata 1982</a>, a paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, which describes <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">McCarthy's</a> indifference to biological models. Kolata quotes McCarthy as writing: "This is AI, so we don't care if it's psychologically real"<cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=PEkqAAAAMAAJ">"Science"</a>. August 1982.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Science&amp;rft.date=1982-08&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DPEkqAAAAMAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArtificial+intelligence" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/>. McCarthy recently reiterated his position at the <a href="/wiki/AI@50" title="AI@50">AI@50</a> conference where he said "Artificial intelligence is not, by definition, simulation of human intelligence" (<a href="#CITEREFMaker2006">Maker 2006</a>).</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_16-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_16-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" class="mw-redirect" title="Neats vs. scruffies">Neats vs. scruffies</a>:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;421–424, 486–489</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp.&#160;168</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFNilsson1983">Nilsson 1983</a>, pp.&#160;10–11</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
Symbolic vs. sub-symbolic AI:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFNilsson1998">Nilsson (1998</a>, p.&#160;7), who uses the term "sub-symbolic".</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-General_intelligence-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-General_intelligence_18-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-General_intelligence_18-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
General intelligence (<a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">strong AI</a>) is discussed in popular introductions to AI:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFKurzweil1999">Kurzweil 1999</a> and <a href="#CITEREFKurzweil2005">Kurzweil 2005</a></li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Workshop" class="mw-redirect" title="Dartmouth Workshop">Dartmouth proposal</a>, under <a href="#Philosophy">Philosophy</a>, below.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-McCorduck&#39;s_thesis-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-McCorduck&#39;s_thesis_20-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-McCorduck&#39;s_thesis_20-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
This is a central idea of <a href="/wiki/Pamela_McCorduck" title="Pamela McCorduck">Pamela McCorduck</a>'s <i>Machines Who Think</i>. She writes: "I like to think of artificial intelligence as the scientific apotheosis of a venerable cultural tradition." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p.&#160;34) "Artificial intelligence in one form or another is an idea that has pervaded Western intellectual history, a dream in urgent need of being realized." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p.&#160;xviii) "Our history is full of attempts—nutty, eerie, comical, earnest, legendary and real—to make artificial intelligences, to reproduce what is the essential us—bypassing the ordinary means. Back and forth between myth and reality, our imaginations supplying what our workshops couldn't, we have engaged for a long time in this odd form of self-reproduction." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p.&#160;3) She traces the desire back to its <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> roots and calls it the urge to "forge the Gods." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;340–400)</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200916-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200916_28-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2009">Russell &amp; Norvig 2009</a>, p.&#160;16.</span>

<li id="cite_note-Dartmouth_conference-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dartmouth_conference_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Workshop" class="mw-redirect" title="Dartmouth Workshop">Dartmouth conference</a>:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;111–136</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp.&#160;47–49, who writes "the conference is generally recognized as the official birthdate of the new science."</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, p.&#160;17, who call the conference "the birth of artificial intelligence."</li>

<li id="cite_note-Definition_of_AI-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Definition_of_AI_57-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Definition_of_AI_57-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
Definition of AI as the study of <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agents" class="mw-redirect" title="Intelligent agents">intelligent agents</a>:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFPooleMackworthGoebel1998">Poole, Mackworth &amp; Goebel 1998</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://people.cs.ubc.ca/~poole/ci/ch1.pdf">p. 1</a>, which provides the version that is used in this article. Note that they use the term "computational intelligence" as a synonym for artificial intelligence.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig (2003)</a> (who prefer the term "rational agent") and write "The whole-agent view is now widely accepted in the field" (<a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, p.&#160;55).</li>

<li id="cite_note-Uncertain_reasoning-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Uncertain_reasoning_86-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
Uncertain reasoning:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp.&#160;452–644,</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFPooleMackworthGoebel1998">Poole, Mackworth &amp; Goebel 1998</a>, pp.&#160;345–395,</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFLugerStubblefield2004">Luger &amp; Stubblefield 2004</a>, pp.&#160;333–381,</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFNilsson1998">Nilsson 1998</a>, chpt. 19</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning_87-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
Psychological evidence of sub-symbolic reasoning:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFWasonShapiro1966">Wason &amp; Shapiro (1966)</a> showed that people do poorly on completely abstract problems, but if the problem is restated to allow the use of intuitive <a href="/wiki/Social_intelligence" title="Social intelligence">social intelligence</a>, performance dramatically improves. (See <a href="/wiki/Wason_selection_task" title="Wason selection task">Wason selection task</a>)</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFKahnemanSlovicTversky1982">Kahneman, Slovic &amp; Tversky (1982)</a> have shown that people are terrible at elementary problems that involve uncertain reasoning. (See <a href="/wiki/List_of_cognitive_biases" title="List of cognitive biases">list of cognitive biases</a> for several examples).</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFLakoffNúñez2000">Lakoff &amp; Núñez (2000)</a> have controversially argued that even our skills at mathematics depend on knowledge and skills that come from "the body", i.e. sensorimotor and perceptual skills. (See <a href="/wiki/Where_Mathematics_Comes_From" title="Where Mathematics Comes From">Where Mathematics Comes From</a>)</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Knowledge_representation-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Knowledge_representation_88-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

Santa Barbara: ACM. pp.&#160;679–682.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=conference&amp;rft.atitle=Automatic+annotation+and+semantic+retrieval+of+video+sequences+using+multimedia+ontologies&amp;rft.btitle=MM+%2706+Proceedings+of+the+14th+ACM+international+conference+on+Multimedia&amp;rft.place=Santa+Barbara&amp;rft.pages=679-682&amp;rft.pub=ACM&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.aulast=Bertini&amp;rft.aufirst=M&amp;rft.au=Del+Bimbo%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Torniai%2C+C&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArtificial+intelligence" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-Qualification_problem-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Qualification_problem_101-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a href="/wiki/Qualification_problem" title="Qualification problem">Qualification problem</a>:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFMcCarthyHayes1969">McCarthy &amp; Hayes 1969</a></li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2011)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li></ul>
While McCarthy was primarily concerned with issues in the logical representation of actions, <a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a> apply the term to the more general issue of default reasoning in the vast network of assumptions underlying all our commonsense knowledge.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_102-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_102-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">

Luger <i>et al.</i> places this under "uncertain reasoning"):

<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is a form of <a href="/wiki/Tom_M._Mitchell" title="Tom M. Mitchell">Tom Mitchell</a>'s widely quoted definition of machine learning: "A computer program is set to learn from an experience <i>E</i> with respect to some task <i>T</i> and some performance measure <i>P</i> if its performance on <i>T</i> as measured by <i>P</i> improves with experience <i>E</i>."</span>

<li id="cite_note-Brain_simulation-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_simulation_150-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_simulation_150-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">Artificial brain</a> arguments: AI requires a simulation of the operation of the human brain
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, p.&#160;957</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp.&#160;271 and 279</li></ul>
A few of the people who make some form of the argument:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFMoravec1988">Moravec 1988</a></li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFKurzweil2005">Kurzweil 2005</a>, p.&#160;262</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFHawkinsBlakeslee2005">Hawkins &amp; Blakeslee 2005</a></li></ul>
The most extreme form of this argument (the brain replacement scenario) was put forward by <a href="/wiki/Clark_Glymour" title="Clark Glymour">Clark Glymour</a> in the mid-1970s and was touched on by <a href="/wiki/Zenon_Pylyshyn" title="Zenon Pylyshyn">Zenon Pylyshyn</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Searle" title="John Searle">John Searle</a> in 1980.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Goertzel, Ben; Lian, Ruiting; Arel, Itamar; de Garis, Hugo; Chen, Shuo (December 2010). <li id="cite_note-Turing_test-332"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Turing_test_332-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
The <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a>:<br />
Turing's original publication:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFTuring1950">Turing 1950</a></li></ul>
Historical influence and philosophical implications:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFHaugeland1985">Haugeland 1985</a>, pp.&#160;6–9</li>

</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Dartmouth_proposal-333"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dartmouth_proposal_333-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

<li id="cite_note-Physical_symbol_system_hypothesis-334"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Physical_symbol_system_hypothesis_334-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
The <a href="/wiki/Physical_symbol_system" title="Physical symbol system">physical symbol systems</a> hypothesis:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFNewellSimon1976">Newell &amp; Simon 1976</a>, p.&#160;116</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p.&#160;153</li>

</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-335"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-335">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
Dreyfus criticized the <a href="/wiki/Necessary_and_sufficient" class="mw-redirect" title="Necessary and sufficient">necessary</a> condition of the <a href="/wiki/Physical_symbol_system" title="Physical symbol system">physical symbol system</a> hypothesis, which he called the "psychological assumption": "The mind can be viewed as a device operating on bits of information according to formal rules." (<a href="#CITEREFDreyfus1992">Dreyfus 1992</a>, p.&#160;156)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Dreyfus&#39;_critique-336"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dreyfus&#39;_critique_336-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a href="/wiki/Dreyfus%27_critique_of_artificial_intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreyfus&#39; critique of artificial intelligence">Dreyfus' critique of artificial intelligence</a>:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFDreyfus1972">Dreyfus 1972</a>, <a href="#CITEREFDreyfusDreyfus1986">Dreyfus &amp; Dreyfus 1986</a></li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp.&#160;120–132</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;211–239</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp.&#160;950–952,</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Gödel_himself-337"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gödel_himself_337-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a href="#CITEREFGödel1951">Gödel 1951</a>: in this lecture, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del" title="Kurt Gödel">Kurt Gödel</a> uses the incompleteness theorem to arrive at the following disjunction: (a) the human mind is not a consistent finite machine, or (b) there exist <a href="/wiki/Diophantine_equations" class="mw-redirect" title="Diophantine equations">Diophantine equations</a> for which it cannot decide whether solutions exist. Gödel finds (b) implausible, and thus seems to have believed the human mind was not equivalent to a finite machine, i.e., its power exceeded that of any finite machine. He recognized that this was only a conjecture, since one could never disprove (b). Yet he considered the disjunctive conclusion to be a "certain fact".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-The_mathematical_objection-338"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-The_mathematical_objection_338-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-358"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-358">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the early 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Colby" title="Kenneth Colby">Kenneth Colby</a> presented a version of Weizenbaum's <a href="/wiki/ELIZA" title="ELIZA">ELIZA</a> known as DOCTOR which he promoted as a serious therapeutic tool. <li id="cite_note-Weizenbaum&#39;s_critique-359"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Weizenbaum&#39;s_critique_359-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a href="/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum" title="Joseph Weizenbaum">Joseph Weizenbaum</a>'s critique of AI:
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFWeizenbaum1976">Weizenbaum 1976</a></li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp.&#160;132–144</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;356–373</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, p.&#160;961</li></ul>
Weizenbaum (the AI researcher who developed the first <a href="/wiki/Chatterbot" class="mw-redirect" title="Chatterbot">chatterbot</a> program, <a href="/wiki/ELIZA" title="ELIZA">ELIZA</a>) argued in 1976 that the misuse of artificial intelligence has the potential to devalue human life.</span>

<li id="cite_note-379"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-379">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steven_Horst&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Steven Horst (page does not exist)">Horst, Steven</a>, (2005) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/computational-mind/">"The Computational Theory of Mind"</a> in <i>The Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Searle&#39;s_strong_AI-380"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Searle&#39;s_strong_AI_380-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
This version is from <a href="#CITEREFSearle1999">Searle (1999)</a>, and is also quoted in <a href="#CITEREFDennett1991">Dennett 1991</a>, p.&#160;435. Searle's original formulation was "The appropriately programmed computer really is a mind, in the sense that computers given the right programs can be literally said to understand and have other cognitive states." (<a href="#CITEREFSearle1980">Searle 1980</a>, p.&#160;1). Strong AI is defined similarly by <a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig (2003</a>, p.&#160;947): "The assertion that machines could possibly act intelligently (or, perhaps better, act as if they were intelligent) is called the 'weak AI' hypothesis by philosophers, and the assertion that machines that do so are actually thinking (as opposed to simulating thinking) is called the 'strong AI' hypothesis."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Chinese_room-381"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Chinese_room_381-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

<li><cite id="CITEREFWeizenbaum1976" class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum" title="Joseph Weizenbaum">Weizenbaum, Joseph</a> (1976). <a href="/wiki/Computer_Power_and_Human_Reason" title="Computer Power and Human Reason"><i>Computer Power and Human Reason</i></a>. San Francisco: W.H. Freeman &amp; Company. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7167-0464-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7167-0464-5"><bdi>978-0-7167-0464-5</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Computer+Power+and+Human+Reason&amp;rft.place=San+Francisco&amp;rft.pub=W.H.+Freeman+%26+Company&amp;rft.date=1976&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7167-0464-5&amp;rft.aulast=Weizenbaum&amp;rft.aufirst=Joseph&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArtificial+intelligence" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFWengMcClellandPentlandSporns2001" class="citation journal">Weng, J.; McClelland; Pentland, A.; Sporns, O.; Stockman, I.; Sur, M.; Thelen, E. (2001). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cse.msu.edu/dl/SciencePaper.pdf">"Autonomous mental development by robots and animals"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Science</i>. <b>291</b> (5504): 599–600. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.291.5504.599">10.1126/science.291.5504.599</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" class="mw-redirect" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11229402">11229402</a>  &#8211; via msu.edu.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Science&amp;rft.atitle=Autonomous+mental+development+by+robots+and+animals&amp;rft.volume=291&amp;rft.issue=5504&amp;rft.pages=599-600&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1126%2Fscience.291.5504.599&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F11229402&amp;rft.aulast=Weng&amp;rft.aufirst=J.&amp;rft.au=McClelland&amp;rft.au=Pentland%2C+A.&amp;rft.au=Sporns%2C+O.&amp;rft.au=Stockman%2C+I.&amp;rft.au=Sur%2C+M.&amp;rft.au=Thelen%2C+E.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.cse.msu.edu%2Fdl%2FSciencePaper.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArtificial+intelligence" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=73" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047268"/><div class="refbegin columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li>DH Author, ‘Why Are There Still So Many Jobs? The History and Future of Workplace Automation’ (2015) 29(3) Journal of Economic Perspectives 3.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Boden" title="Margaret Boden">Boden, Margaret</a>, <i>Mind As Machine</i>, <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>, 2006.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Cukier" title="Kenneth Cukier">Cukier, Kenneth</a>, "Ready for Robots?  How to Think about the Future of AI", <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Affairs" title="Foreign Affairs">Foreign Affairs</a></i>, vol. 98, no. 4 (July/August 2019), pp. 192–98.  <a href="/wiki/George_Dyson_(science_historian)" title="George Dyson (science historian)">George Dyson</a>, historian of computing, writes (in what might be called "Dyson's Law") that "Any system simple enough to be understandable will not be complicated enough to behave intelligently, while any system complicated enough to behave intelligently will be too complicated to understand." (p. 197.)  Computer scientist <a href="/wiki/Alex_Pentland" title="Alex Pentland">Alex Pentland</a> writes:  "Current <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">AI machine-learning</a> <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> are, at their core, dead simple stupid.  They work, but they work by brute force." (p. 198.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pedro_Domingos" title="Pedro Domingos">Domingos, Pedro</a>, "Our Digital Doubles:  AI will serve our species, not control it", <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>, vol. 319, no. 3 (September 2018), pp.&#160;88–93.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alison_Gopnik" title="Alison Gopnik">Gopnik, Alison</a>, "Making AI More Human:  Artificial intelligence has staged a revival by starting to incorporate what we know about how children learn", <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>, vol. 316, no. 6 (June 2017), pp.&#160;60–65.</li>
<li>Johnston, John (2008) <i>The Allure of Machinic Life: Cybernetics, Artificial Life, and the New AI</i>, MIT Press</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gary_Marcus" title="Gary Marcus">Marcus, Gary</a>, "Am I Human?: Researchers need new ways to distinguish artificial intelligence from the natural kind", <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>, vol. 316, no. 3 (March 2017), pp.&#160;58–63.  A stumbling block to AI has been an incapacity for reliable <a href="/wiki/Disambiguation" class="mw-redirect" title="Disambiguation">disambiguation</a>.  An example is the "pronoun disambiguation problem":  a machine has no way of determining to whom or what a <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronoun</a> in a sentence refers. (p. 61.)</li>
<li>E McGaughey, 'Will Robots Automate Your Job Away? Full Employment, Basic Income, and Economic Democracy' (2018) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3044448">SSRN, part 2(3)</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_Musser" title="George Musser">George Musser</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Artificial_Imagination" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial Imagination">Artificial Imagination</a>:  How machines could learn <a href="/wiki/Creativity" title="Creativity">creativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_sense" title="Common sense">common sense</a>, among other human qualities", <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>, vol. 320, no. 5 (May 2019), pp. 58–63.</li>
<li>Myers, Courtney Boyd ed. (2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.forbes.com/2009/06/22/singularity-robots-computers-opinions-contributors-artificial-intelligence-09_land.html">"The AI Report"</a>. <i>Forbes</i> June 2009</li>
<li><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Bertram_Raphael" title="Bertram Raphael">Raphael, Bertram</a> (1976). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/thinkingcomputer00raph"><i>The Thinking Computer</i></a>. W.H.Freeman and Company. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7167-0723-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7167-0723-3"><bdi>978-0-7167-0723-3</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Thinking+Computer&amp;rft.pub=W.H.Freeman+and+Company&amp;rft.date=1976&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7167-0723-3&amp;rft.aulast=Raphael&amp;rft.aufirst=Bertram&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fthinkingcomputer00raph&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArtificial+intelligence" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li>Scharre, Paul, "Killer Apps:  The Real Dangers of an AI Arms Race", <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Affairs" title="Foreign Affairs">Foreign Affairs</a></i>, vol. 98, no. 3 (May/June 2019), pp. 135–44.  "Today's AI technologies are powerful but unreliable.  Rules-based systems cannot deal with circumstances their programmers did not anticipate.  Learning systems are limited by the data on which they were trained.  AI failures have already led to tragedy.  Advanced autopilot features in cars, although they perform well in some circumstances, have driven cars without warning into trucks, concrete barriers, and parked cars.  In the wrong situation, AI systems go from supersmart to superdumb in an instant.  When an enemy is trying to manipulate and hack an AI system, the risks are even greater."  (p. 140.)</li>
<li><cite class="citation journal">Serenko, Alexander (2010). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.aserenko.com/papers/JOI_Serenko_AI_Journal_Ranking_Published.pdf">"The development of an AI journal ranking based on the revealed preference approach"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Journal of Informetrics</i>. <b>4</b> (4): 447–459. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.joi.2010.04.001">10.1016/j.joi.2010.04.001</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Informetrics&amp;rft.atitle=The+development+of+an+AI+journal+ranking+based+on+the+revealed+preference+approach&amp;rft.volume=4&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=447-459&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.joi.2010.04.001&amp;rft.aulast=Serenko&amp;rft.aufirst=Alexander&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.aserenko.com%2Fpapers%2FJOI_Serenko_AI_Journal_Ranking_Published.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArtificial+intelligence" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li><cite class="citation journal">Serenko, Alexander; Michael Dohan (2011). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.aserenko.com/papers/JOI_AI_Journal_Ranking_Serenko.pdf">"Comparing the expert survey and citation impact journal ranking methods: Example from the field of Artificial Intelligence"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Journal of Informetrics</i>. <b>5</b> (4): 629–649. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.joi.2011.06.002">10.1016/j.joi.2011.06.002</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Informetrics&amp;rft.atitle=Comparing+the+expert+survey+and+citation+impact+journal+ranking+methods%3A+Example+from+the+field+of+Artificial+Intelligence&amp;rft.volume=5&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=629-649&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.joi.2011.06.002&amp;rft.aulast=Serenko&amp;rft.aufirst=Alexander&amp;rft.au=Michael+Dohan&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.aserenko.com%2Fpapers%2FJOI_AI_Journal_Ranking_Serenko.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArtificial+intelligence" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li>Sun, R. &amp; Bookman, L. (eds.), <i>Computational Architectures: Integrating Neural and Symbolic Processes</i>. Kluwer Academic Publishers, Needham, MA. 1994.</li>
<li><cite class="citation web">Tom Simonite (29 December 2014). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.technologyreview.com/news/533686/2014-in-computing-breakthroughs-in-artificial-intelligence/">"2014 in Computing: Breakthroughs in Artificial Intelligence"</a>. <i>MIT Technology Review</i>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=MIT+Technology+Review&amp;rft.atitle=2014+in+Computing%3A+Breakthroughs+in+Artificial+Intelligence&amp;rft.date=2014-12-29&amp;rft.au=Tom+Simonite&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.technologyreview.com%2Fnews%2F533686%2F2014-in-computing-breakthroughs-in-artificial-intelligence%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArtificial+intelligence" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adam_Tooze" title="Adam Tooze">Tooze, Adam</a>, "Democracy and Its Discontents", <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Review_of_Books" title="The New York Review of Books">The New York Review of Books</a></i>, vol. LXVI, no. 10 (6 June 2019), pp. 52–53, 56–57.  "Democracy has no clear answer for the mindless operation of <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucratic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technological power</a>.  We may indeed be witnessing its extension in the form of <a class="mw-selflink selflink">artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>.  Likewise, after decades of dire warning, the <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmental problem</a> remains fundamentally unaddressed.... Bureaucratic overreach and environmental catastrophe are precisely the kinds of slow-moving existential challenges that democracies deal with very badly.... Finally, there is the threat du jour:  <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporations</a> and the technologies they promote."  (pp. 56–57.)</li></ul>
